lawbreaker**]{.mark}.]{.underline}

### 2AC\-\--China Thumper

#### China abuses UNCLOS provisions for military expansion

Elizabeth C. **Parker**, 16-10-20**24**, \"Policing the Strait: How
China's Policing of the Taiwan Strait Violates the U.N. Convention on
the Law of the Sea,\" Digital Commons @ American University Washington
College of Law,
<https://digitalcommons.wcl.american.edu/auilr/vol39/iss4/7/>\
As tensions continue to grow across the Taiwan Strait, **[[China is
using domestic maritime law to justify an expansion of police power
in]{.underline}]{.mark}** the international waters of the **[[Taiwan
Strait in violation of international law]{.underline}]{.mark}**. This
Comment will argue that the **[[People's Republic of China has
violated]{.underline}]{.mark}** the UN Convention on the Law of the Sea
("**[[UNCLOS]{.underline}]{.mark}**") **[[by implementing
non-resource-related police power in waters meant only for resource
exploration and management.]{.underline}]{.mark}** In comparing the
language of China's domestic maritime law with that of UNCLOS, this
Comment will show that the **[[People's Republic of
China]{.underline}]{.mark}** has used domestic law to reinterpret and
**[[obfuscate its obligations under UNCLOS]{.underline}]{.mark}**. As a
result, the **[[freedom of navigation granted by UNCLOS to all States
has been restricted by China's policing.]{.underline}]{.mark}** To
prevent China from using its domestic law to assert increasing control
over the Strait, the Tribunal of the Law of the Sea should issue an
advisory opinion clarifying the purpose of Exclusive Economic Zones and
denouncing China's noncompliance with UNCLOS. Additionally, foreign
States should continue to exercise freedom of navigation through the
Taiwan Strait and push China to limit its police activity of the Strait.

#### China consistently violates UNCLOS unchecked. 

Fata Mazaya **Zhafir**, 12-9-20**23**, \"Understanding the Lack of Legal
Action Against China's South China Sea Assertions,\" Modern Diplomacy,
https://moderndiplomacy.eu/2025/02/25/understanding-the-lack-of-legal-action-against-chinas-south-china-sea-assertions/

The South China Sea is a strategic body of water bordering Brunei
Darussalam, Malaysia, Indonesia, the Philippines, Singapore, Thailand,
Vietnam, and China. As one of the busiest trade routes in the world and
home to a wealth of marine and mineral resources, the South China Sea
holds great economic and geostrategic importance. **[[Countries have
wrangled over territory in the South China Sea for centuries, but
tension has grown in recent years]{.underline}]{.mark}**. In particular,
**[[China's sweeping claims that violate]{.underline}]{.mark}** the
United Nations Convention on the Law of the Sea
**[[(UNCLOS]{.underline}]{.mark}**), which **[[include sovereignty
claims over land parcels and their adjacent
waters]{.underline}]{.mark}**, have angered competing claimants like
Vietnam, the Philippines, Taiwan, Malaysia, and Brunei (BBC, 2024). The
maritime disputes in the South China Sea are far from being localized
conflicts; rather, they hold both regional and global implications,
impacting the delicate balance of power and international law. As stated
before, many countries are affected by the act of China, but other than
the Philippines, **[[there are no major legal actions against
China]{.underline}]{.mark}**. Why don't countries take legal actions
regarding China's violation of UNCLOS in the South China Sea? The United
Nations Convention on the Law of the Sea (UNCLOS) is an international
agreement that establishes guidelines for businesses, the environment,
and the management of marine natural resources (IMO, 2012). This
agreement plays an important role in the way China is claiming
territories. We have to acknowledge the role of UNCLOS in assisting to
provide governance arrangements for the maritime domain in certain
circumstances and note that China intends to work constructively over
the coming decade to help refine it (Shi, 2015). However, a Japanese
writer named Atsuko Kanehara (2018) argues that China's sovereignty in
the South China Sea is not a subject that UNCLOS should be adjudicating
because China's claim is based on historic rights that are defined under
a regime independent of UNCLOS. Moreover, it contends that the continued
insistence of some countries in attempting to use UNCLOS to frame the
debate about China's claim in the South China Sea is not only incorrect
but is also increasing the risk of regional instability. In 2013, the
Philippines, under UNCLOS, initiated an arbitral procedure against
China. In this respect, the Permanent Court of Arbitration (PCA), on
July 12, 2016, issued its award, which was with the Philippines,
declaring that significant parts of China's claims, its nine-dash line
and new land reclamation activities, were illegal. Seeing many
perspectives regarding this heated state of the South China Sea, there's
a lack of information given as to the reason why other countries
impacted by China's action did not take legal action against China;
thus, the research question of this writing was birthed.** **Despite
many theories in the International Relations realm, the theory of
neorealism will come to play in this writing. Neorealism is a theory
that emphasizes the role of power politics in international relations,
which sees competition and conflict as enduring features and sees
limited potential for cooperation (Lobell, 2017). Neorealism was a
purely systemic theory, one that focused on the characteristics of the
international system structure, rather than the nature of individuals or
states. Rather, it posited a minimalist concern with survival in an
anarchic setting as the only necessary condition. Through the neorealism
theory, this writing will discuss in depth the main reasons how
neorealism theory can navigate the tension in South China Sea regarding
China's violation of UNCLOS.**[[Several countries in the South China
Sea]{.underline}]{.mark}**, both individually and collectively,
**[[pointed out China's serious violations of
UNCLOS]{.underline}]{.mark}** in this region. **[[Vietnam has condemned
China's]{.underline}]{.mark}** interference in its oil and gas
exploration activities as continually claiming territorial jurisdiction
over areas belonging to Vietnam. This also includes those cases where
the Chinese Coast Guards patrolled Vietnamese territories without
permission, in blatant disregard for the provisions under UNCLOS that
ensure marine preservation and prevent damage to fragile ecosystems.
**[[ASEAN members have also collectively voiced their
unease]{.underline}]{.mark}** concerning unilateral acts by China,
calling for compliance with international law, in addition to promoting
a rules-based maritime order. Though the individual reactions may vary
because some have more or less engagement with China, the sentiment
carries the shared frustration of blatant disregard of UNCLOS by China
and the destabilization of the region as a result. A researcher named Ky
Nguyen (2018) mentioned that the international system of neorealism is
anarchic, meaning no overarching authority enforces the rules and judges
their disputes. In this kind of system, states are dependent upon their
capabilities to secure themselves and their interest. He added that the
**[[countries surrounding the South China Sea do believe that taking
legal action against a powerful state would lead to military and
economic retaliation]{.underline}]{.mark}**. Therefore,
**[[they]{.underline}]{.mark}** tend towards self-help measures and
**[[remain hostile to legal actions as well because they fear that legal
steps may provoke a more crushing retaliatory response from China that
would cast a bad effect on their security
situation.]{.underline}]{.mark}** He added that neorealists contend that
states are more concerned about relative gains---that is, their own
power and security compared to others---than with absolute gains. This
mindset discourages cooperation if it might enhance the power of a
rival. It can thus be ruled out in the South China Sea by any nation
that sees it as a tool to provoke China to force it into aggressive
actions against them. Instead, such nations might choose to employ a
status quo that offers a situation of less conflict while still
safeguarding their interests. Increased military ability and an
assertive foreign policy have brought China to a hegemonic position in
the region of Southeast Asia (Tokuchi, 2022). Knowing this, neorealists
would suggest that weaker states will usually seek to balance against a
dominant power, rather than confront such a power. Countries may prefer
to negotiate through diplomatic means and soft bargaining through
multilateral discussions within ASEAN rather than push through with
legal action perceived to be confrontational. In doing so, the countries
aim at balancing to avoid head-on conflict with China while still
putting across their claims. The neorealist view also highlights the
importance of alliances and strategic calculations in international
relations. Smaller countries can decide that taking legal action could
further isolate them from potential allies or even destabilize regional
cooperation. They may prefer to negotiate informally or to seek support
from greater powers, such as the United States, that exercise freedom of
navigation operations but themselves do not claim territorial rights on
certain regions. Lastly, the reason countries hesitate to take legal
action against China roots from concerns about security, relative gains,
power dynamics, and strategic considerations regarding alliances; these
concerns make countries prioritize stability and a sense of
self-preservation over potentially confrontational legal battles. On top
of it, the **[[ambiguous interpretation of
UNCLOS]{.underline}]{.mark}**, added to China's historical claims, makes
legal action not so clear-cut. In the final analysis, such dynamics
underline a strategic choice of diplomacy over legal action among
smaller nations in navigating the complex geopolitical landscape of the
region.

that preserves the rules-based legal order that has brought peace and

### 2AC\-\--No Impact

#### International law is [meaningless]{.underline}

**Hiken and Hiken 12** \[Luke Hiken and Marti Hiken, National Lawyers
Guild. Military Law Task Force, The Impotence of International Law",
Foreign Policy in Focus, 7/17/2012,
https://fpif.org/the_impotence_of_international_law/\] //adiiiiG

[Whenever a lawyer or historian describes how a particular action
"violates international law" many people **stop listening or reading
further**.]{.underline} It is a bit alienating to hear the words "this
action constitutes a violation of international law" time and time again
-- and especially at the end of a debate when a speaker has no other
arguments available. The statement is inevitably followed by: "...and it
is a war crime and it denies people their human
rights." [A [plethora of international]{.mark} [law **violations are
perpetrated by every major power**]{.mark}** in the world [each
day]{.mark}**, and thus,
the [empty invocation]{.mark} of international law [does
nothing]{.mark} but reinforce our own sense of impotence and
helplessness in the face of international lawlessness.]{.underline}

[The [U]{.mark}nited [S]{.mark}tates, alone, and [on a **daily
basis **violates]{.mark} every principle of international
law ever envisioned: **unprovoked [wars of aggression]{.mark}**;
**unmanned [drone attacks]{.mark}**; **[tortures]{.mark} and [renditions]{.mark}**; **[assassinations]{.mark} of
our alleged "enemies"**; **[sales
of nuclear weapons]{.mark}**; **[destabilization]{.mark} of unfriendly
governments**; creating **the largest [prison]{.mark}** population in
the world -- the [list is **virtually endless**]{.mark}.]{.underline}

Obviously one would wish that there existed a body of international
law that could put an end to these abuses, but such [[laws exist
in **theory**]{.mark}, not in practice]{.underline}. Each time a legal
scholar points out the particular treaties being ignored
by the superpowers (and everyone else) the only appropriate response
is "so what!" or "they always say that." If [[there is no
enforcement mechanism]{.mark} to prevent the violations, [and no
military force]{.mark} with the power [to intervene]{.mark}]{.underline}
on behalf of those victimized by the violations, what possible good does
it do to invoke principles of "truth and justice" that border on
fantasy?

The assumption is that by invoking human rights principles, legal
scholars hope to reinforce the importance of and need for such a body of
law. Yet, in reality, the [[invocation means
nothing]{.underline}]{.mark} at the present time, and goes nowhere. In
the real world, it would be nice to focus on suggestions that are
enforceable, and have some potential to prevent the atrocities taking
place around the globe. Scholars who invoke international law principles
would do well to add to their analysis, some form of action or conduct
at the present time that might prevent such violations from happening.
Alternatively, praying for rain sounds as effective and rational as
citing international legal principles to a lawless president, and
his ruthless military.

#### Non-compliance means UNCLOS is [meaningless]{.underline}

**Churchill 12** \[Robin Churchill, Dundee Law School, University of
Dundee United Kingdom, January 1st 2012, "The Persisting Problem of
Non-compliance with the Law of the Sea Convention: Disorder in the
Oceans," The International Journal of Marine and Coastal Law,
https://brill.com/view/journals/estu/27/4/article-p813_15.xml\]
//adiiiiG

Abstract

There is [[widespread and persisting non-compliance]{.underline}]{.mark}
in relation to many [[of]{.mark} the provisions of the [UN
C]{.mark}onvention on the [L]{.mark}aw [o]{.mark}f the
[S]{.mark}ea.]{.underline} This is a matter of serious concern because
it [[undermines]{.mark} the [integrity and legitimacy of the
Convention]{.mark}]{.underline}, [causes disputes and harms the marine
environment]{.underline}. To remedy the situation, more use should be
made of existing mechanisms to induce compliance, especially the
possibilities of compulsory dispute settlement under Part XV of the
Convention, retorsion and counter-measures, and by developing compliance
mechanisms for other treaties that indirectly help to promote compliance
with the Convention. In some cases assistance in capacity building may
also be desirable.

Keywords: compliance; counter-measures; dispute
settlement; non-compliance

Introduction

The 30th anniversary of the opening for signature of the UN Convention
on the Law of the Sea (LOSC or the Convention) will no doubt be marked
by many positive and complimentary remarks being made about the
Convention. However, at the risk of being a party-pooper, I want to
[focus here on a negative aspect]{.underline}: [the problem of
**persisting and widespread non-compliance** with the
Convention]{.underline}. I begin by giving some examples of such
non-compliance and explaining why I consider it to be a problem that
merits serious attention. I then suggest some ways in which the current
situation could be improved.

Non-compliance with the Convention

For reasons of space, I cannot attempt here any detailed or
comprehensive survey of non-compliance with the LOSC, nor is it
necessary to my case to do so. It is sufficient to give a number of
varying and persisting examples to show that non-compliance continues to
be a problem.

[[Some States]{.mark} parties, including Bangladesh, Egypt, Myanmar and
Vietnam, have [drawn straight baselines]{.mark} in ways [that **do not
meet**]{.mark} the requirements of [Article 7]{.mark}]{.underline}, even
on the most generous interpretation of the admittedly imprecise
provisions of that Article.1

[[Four States]{.mark} parties (Benin, Philippines, Somalia and Togo)
still [claim a territorial sea]{.mark} [**with a breadth in excess** of
the 12 nautical miles]{.mark} permitted by Article 3.]{.underline}

[[12 States]{.mark} parties, including China, Haiti, India, Pakistan and
Saudi Arabia, have [included security as one of the matters]{.mark} in
respect of [which they claim to exercise]{.mark} jurisdiction in their
contiguous zones, [contrary to Article 33]{.mark}.]{.underline}

[A [few States parties]{.mark}, including Japan]{.underline}, have
[[sought to delimit an]{.mark} exclusive economic zone ([EEZ]{.mark})
[and continental shelf]{.mark} from uninhabitable rocks, [contrary to
Article 121]{.mark}(3).]{.underline}

[A [considerable number of]{.mark} flag [State]{.mark} parties [are in
breach of]{.mark} their obligation under [Article 94]{.mark} to exercise
effective jurisdiction and control in respect of the seaworthiness of
ships having their nationality]{.underline}, as revealed by the record
of inspections and detentions of unseaworthy ships carried out by port
States under various regional Memoranda of Understanding (MoU) on port
State control. In particular, such States include those on the black
list of flag States, and possibly even those on the grey list, published
each year by the Paris and Tokyo MoUs.2

The biennial reports on the State of World Fisheries and Aquaculture
published by the FAO show that for the past decade or more nearly 30% of
fish stocks are over-exploited.3 This indicates that some coastal States
parties (notably the European Union (EU))4 are in breach of their
obligation under Article 61(2) to ensure that the maintenance of the
living resources of their EEZs is not endangered by over-exploitation;
and that some States parties are in breach of their obligation under
Articles 117--119 to conserve the living resources of the high seas.
Furthermore, it has been estimated that as much as one-third of the
total global marine fish catch is taken illegally.5 Not all such
illegality is necessarily a breach of the LOSC, but a good deal
certainly is.

[A [number of States]{.mark} parties [are in breach of their obligations
under Article 194]{.mark}(5) by [failing to take]{.mark} the necessary
[measures to protect]{.mark} and preserve rare or fragile
[ecosystems]{.mark}, for example by permitting fishing using explosives
in the vicinity of tropical coral reefs or by permitting bottom trawling
on seamounts and areas of cold-water coral reefs in their
EEZs.]{.underline}

Why Non-compliance Matters

Some may seek to downplay the significance of non-compliance with the
LOSC by arguing that a certain level of non-compliance is to be expected
in any legal system: as long as it is kept within reasonable bounds,
there need be no undue concern. I believe that this is too sanguine a
view. [There are [just over 160 parties]{.mark}]{.underline} to the
LOSC, [**[at least one-third]{.mark}** of which ([and]{.mark} quite
possibly [more]{.mark}) **[are in breach of at least one]{.mark}
[significant provision]{.mark}** [of the LOSC]{.mark}]{.underline}. Such
a degree of non-compliance [undermines the integrity and legitimacy of
the LOSC.]{.underline} Furthermore, non-compliance [[provokes disputes,
denies States parties some of their LOSC rights, threatens good order at
sea, and harms the marine environment.]{.underline}]{.mark}

How Could Non-compliance with the LOSC Be More Effectively Addressed?

The first and most obvious answer is by making more use of the
mechanisms that already exist for trying to induce or coerce States
parties into complying with the LOSC. The LOSC itself provides three
such mechanisms. The first is the Commission on the Limits of the
Continental Shelf (CLCS), which, in examining States' submissions
relating to the establishment of the outer limit of the continental
shelf beyond 200 nautical miles and making recommendations thereon, is
designed to prevent non-compliance with Article 76 of the LOSC. The CLCS
does not, however, pass opinion on ancillary issues of compliance, such
as the validity of baselines from which 200- and 350-nautical-mile
limits are delimited or whether a continental shelf is being claimed
from Article 121(3)-type islands; nor does it have the expertise to do
so. The second mechanism is the International Seabed Authority (ISA),
whose duties include seeking to ensure compliance with the rules
governing the exploration and exploitation of mineral resources in the
Area. The CLCS and ISA are of little relevance to the present discussion
because up to now non-compliance in relation to the matters with which
they deal has not been a problem.

Of far greater relevance is the third compliance mechanism of the LOSC,
the system of compulsory dispute settlement found in Part XV. This has
so far been used comparatively little to deal with issues of
non-compliance: only 17 cases alleging non-compliance by a State party
have been brought, of which eight concern the failure to release fishing
vessels promptly on payment of a bond or other security. Only two of the
cases (the Southern Bluefin Tuna and Swordfish cases)6 concern any of
the instances of non-compliance referred to earlier, and in neither of
those cases was there a judgment on the merits. Use of Part XV presents
a striking contrast with use of the World Trade Organization's (WTO)
Dispute Settlement Understanding (DSU),7 which also provides for
compulsory dispute settlement and which came into force only a few weeks
after the LOSC. No less than 440 cases have so far been brought under
the DSU. Part of the reason for the stark difference in numbers is that
usually far more, economically speaking, will hang on a WTO dispute than
on LOSC non-compliance; and the former, unlike the latter, invariably
involves direct injury to other States.

States parties to the LOSC may have been put off using Part XV to
challenge non-compliance by the cost and bother of bringing a case,
especially where Annex VII would be the forum. The latter is likely to
be the situation because of the small number of States that have chosen
the International Tribunal for the Law of the Sea (ITLOS) as their
preferred means of settlement---although where a matter has been
referred to arbitration, it can always be transferred by agreement to
the ITLOS to reduce the cost, as has already happened in some cases.
States may also be discouraged from litigating because some kinds of
non-compliance may fall within the matters excepted from compulsory
dispute settlement under Articles 297 and 298, and because of
uncertainty as to whether they have sufficient legal interest to bring a
case. The latter ought not to be a problem: Article 48 of the
International Law Commission's (ILC) Draft Articles on State
Responsibility8 suggests that it should be possible for any party to the
LOSC to challenge non-compliance by another. It is quite surprising that
more use has not been made of Part XV because it provides the
possibility for a more decisive outcome than most other means of
challenging non-compliance. A good example involves Japan's claim to be
able to establish an EEZ and continental shelf from Okinotorishima. This
is clearly contrary to Article 121(3), as at high tide Okinotorishima
comprises two isolated lumps of rock, one the size of a bedroom, the
other scarcely larger than a double bed. China and South Korea have
mounted a diplomatic campaign to challenge Japan's claim. So far, they
have tried, without success, to put the matter on the agenda of the
annual meetings of the States Parties to the LOSC and of the ISA. They
have also objected to Japan's submission to the CLCS in respect of
Okinotorishima. This has resulted in the CLCS deciding to defer a
decision on this aspect of Japan's submission until the three States
have resolved their dispute over the matter.9 It would have been much
quicker, and would have produced a more decisive result, if China and
South Korea had referred the matter for judicial settlement. Indeed,
they still could do so.

Apart from the non-compliance mechanisms of the LOSC, States can also
seek to induce compliance by utilising the traditional mechanisms of
international law, namely protest, retorsion, counter-measures and
action under Article 60 of the Vienna Convention on the Law of Treaties
(VCLT).10 Numerous protests have been made with respect to
jurisdictional claims considered not to be in conformity with the LOSC.
They rarely seem to have had any effect, although just occasionally (as
with the modification by Philippines of its non-complying archipelagic
claims) protests do appear to have persuaded a State to conform. Some
use has been made of retorsion, for example the trade measures employed
by Chile against EU vessels fishing in the south-east Pacific (which
gave rise to the Swordfish litigation) and the EU's ban on imports of
IUU-caught fish, insofar as such measures may be justified under Article
XX of the GATT.11 If they cannot, they would in theory be
counter-measures, although there is considerable doubt over whether WTO
members may impose trade restrictions as counter-measures. There would
appear to be scope for a greater use or threat of counter-measures to
induce compliance with the LOSC than has occurred hitherto, although
such action may be inhibited by the fear that counter-measures may have
adverse effects on the State utilising them and by uncertainty as to
whether they may be invoked by non-injured States (a matter that the
ILC's Draft Articles on State Responsibility leave open). In theory
States could also seek to induce compliance by threatening to terminate
or suspend the LOSC vis-à-vis a non-complying State party for material
breach, in accordance with Article 60 VCLT. However, it is doubtful
whether many, if any, instances of non-compliance with the LOSC amount
to material breach within the meaning of Article 60. Even if the LOSC
was suspended, the State at which such an action was directed would
continue to enjoy most of its LOSC rights under customary international
law.

The possibility of using the UN Security Council to induce compliance
with the LOSC has recently been revealed. In one of its many resolutions
on Somalia (which continues to claim a 200-nautical-mile territorial
sea) and Somali piracy, adopted under Chapter VII of the UN Charter, the
Security Council "emphasizes the importance of the earliest possible
delimitation of Somalia's maritime spaces in accordance with the \[Law
of the Sea\] Convention."12 It is not inconceivable that in future where
a threat or breach of the peace arose out of a State asserting
jurisdiction in a way contrary to the LOSC, the Security Council would
call on the State concerned to amend its legislation to conform to the
LOSC.

Mechanisms in other treaties may help to induce compliance with the
LOSC. It has already been mentioned that the Paris MoU has, indirectly,
identified flag States in breach of Article 94. The Paris MoU has
recently been amended in ways that should put greater pressure on flag
States to improve their performance and thus promote compliance with
Article 94. Ships that have the nationality of States on the black or
grey lists and that have been detained in the past are to be banned,
temporarily or permanently, from entering Paris MOU ports. A shipowner
whose ships have the nationality of a State on the black or grey lists
might well consider re-registering such ships under the flag of a State
on the white list in order to avoid the risk of their being banned from
Paris MOU ports. Such a move would reduce the income from ship
registration that the previous flag State received. That might persuade
the latter to exercise its responsibilities more diligently so that it
was removed from the black and grey lists. In doing so, it would
probably have brought itself into compliance with Article 94. Other
examples could be given involving regional fisheries management
organizations and marine pollution conventions, but lack of space
precludes further consideration here.

The discussion so far has been all about using existing mechanisms to
seek to persuade non-complying States to conform to the LOSC. Could new
mechanisms be created for this purpose? There is no point considering
mechanisms that would require the negotiation and adoption of a new
treaty because there is likely to be little enthusiasm among States
parties for this. There are, however, one or two possibilities that do
not require a new treaty. The first would be for the Meeting of States
Parties to the LOSC to take on the role of monitoring compliance. The
Meeting itself is too unwieldy and political a body to undertake such a
task. It could, however, delegate it to a committee. It might be
objected that the Meeting has no competence to establish such a
committee. However, the Meeting has already shown itself capable of
taking decisions where there was no clear authority in the LOSC to do
so, notably when it extended the time limit for making submissions to
the CLCS.13 However, on that occasion there was a clear consensus to
take action. There is unlikely to be such a consensus for establishing a
compliance committee, as many developed States are opposed to widening
the role of the Meeting. In any case, even if such a committee were
established, it is not clear that it could take any action to induce
compliance other than by naming and shaming States that it had found to
be in non-compliance and urging them to comply. Another possibility
would be for the UN Secretary-General to highlight instances of
non-compliance in his annual reports on oceans and the law of the
sea.14 In the past he has occasionally done so in general terms.
However, he is unlikely to want to do so by identifying States by name
as this is likely to be perceived as political interference and as
compromising his neutrality.

So far the discussion has been all about negative means, i.e., ways of
seeking to induce States to comply with the LOSC by threatening some
form of sanction. But there may also be some scope for positive
measures. Some non-compliance may be due to a lack of adequate capacity.
For example, the failure of many West African States to prevent the
over-exploitation of the fishery resources of their EEZs is probably due
to a lack of the necessary capacity to take adequate enforcement
measures against fleets from East Asia and Europe fishing illegally in
their waters. In some cases, therefore, help in capacity building may be
the most appropriate way to promote compliance with the LOSC.

Conclusion

[[In 1982]{.underline}]{.mark} it may have been reasonable, if perhaps
somewhat optimistic, to hope that [the [LOSC would]{.mark}, in the words
of its preamble, [establish a "legal order]{.mark} for . . . the oceans
which . . . will [promote the peaceful use]{.mark}s of the seas and
oceans, . . . the [conservation of]{.mark} their [living resources
and]{.mark} the . . . [preservation of the]{.mark} marine
[environment]{.mark}." [Thirty years later]{.mark} it is **[clear
that]{.mark} the [LOSC has failed to achieve those]{.mark}
[goa]{.mark}**[ls]{.mark}.]{.underline} This is in part [[due to
continuing non-compliance]{.underline}]{.mark} with many of its
provisions. Such non-compliance [is a matter of serious
concern]{.underline} for all the reasons suggested earlier. It
could---and should---be addressed by States parties making more use of
Part XV of the LOSC (perhaps non-governmental organizations could
persuade or help States to bring test cases); by considering more use of
retorsion and counter-measures; and by developing compliance mechanisms
for other treaties that indirectly help to promote compliance with the
LOSC. In some cases assistance in capacity building may also be
appropriate.

## AT Bioprospecting

### 2AC\-\--No AMR Impact

#### New antibiotics fight back against AMR

**Smriti '25** \[Mallapaty Smriti; Marth 26, 2025; Smriti is the Editor
in Chief of Nature; she studied sex determination at the MRC Laboratory
of Molecular Biology, Cambridge, UK; she serves on the advisory board of
the University of Oxford Centre for Personalised Medicine and the board
of directors for GESDA; she is a fellow of the International Science
Council; "New antibiotic that kills drug-resistant bacteria discovered
in technician's garden"; Nature;
https://www.nature.com/articles/d41586-025-00945-z; accessed
07-16-2025\] NT-LY

[Researchers have discovered a **new antibiotic molecule that targets a
broad range of** disease-causing **bacteria**]{.underline} --- [even
strains resistant to commercial drugs]{.underline} --- and is not toxic
to human cells1.

The molecule was found in soil samples collected from a laboratory
technician's garden. The discovery shows that "there is terrifically
interesting stuff hiding in plain sight", says Kim Lewis, a
microbiologist at Northeastern University in Boston, Massachusetts, who
was not involved in the research. "Kudos to them that they knew what to
look for."

[The latest molecule targets bacteria's protein-making factory, the
ribosome]{.underline}, in a way that other antibiotic drugs don't. The
[ribosome is an attractive antibiotic target because **bacteria don't
easily develop resistance to drugs targeting the structure**, adds
Lewis.]{.underline}

The search for new antibiotics is necessary because bacteria
acquire resistance to existing drugs with continued use. In 2021,
bacterial resistance to antimicrobial drugs was associated with 1.1
million deaths globally, a figure that could increase to 1.9 million by
2050.

### 1AR\-\--No AMR Impact

#### Antibiotics are being found that can counteract bacteria from developing resistance

**Joel '25** \[Lucas Van Wyk Joel; February 24, 2025; Joel is a
professional journalist who specializes in natural and physical
sciences; he is a senior science writer for the University of
California; he is a regular contributor to the Science Times section of
the New York Times; he is a fellow for the National Science Foundation
of Graduate Students; "**UC Irvine scientists invent new drug candidates
to treat antibiotic-resistant bacteria"; UC Irvine News;
https://news.uci.edu/2025/02/24/uc-irvine-scientists-invent-new-drug-candidates-to-treat-antibiotic-resistant-bacteria/;
accessed 07-16-2025**\] NT-LY

There's an arms race in medicine -- scientists design drugs to treat
lethal bacterial infections, but bacteria can evolve defenses to those
drugs, sending the researchers back to square one. In the *Journal of
the American Chemical Society, *a University of California, Irvine-led
team [describes the development of a drug candidate that can stop
bacteria before they have a chance to cause harm.]{.underline}

"The issue with antibiotics is this crisis of antibiotic resistance,"
said Sophia Padilla, a Ph.D. candidate in chemistry and lead author of
the new study. "When it comes to antibiotics, bacteria can evolve
defenses against them -- they're becoming stronger and always getting
better at protecting themselves."

About 35,000 people in the U.S. die each year from antibiotic-resistant
bacterial infections from pathogens like *Staphylococcus*, while about
2.8 million people suffer from bacteria-related illnesses.

"It's a big problem," said James Nowick, a Distinguished Professor of
chemistry at UC Irvine who co-led the study.

The [team designed a new family of antibiotics that's a variation of an
existing drug called vancomycin]{.underline}, which is used as a last
resort for extremely ill patients. The new version of [vancomycin
targets, bonds to and **renders inactive two different parts of** a
molecule on the surfaces of **pathogenic bacteria.**]{.underline}

Nowick likens the process to grabbing the bacteria with both hands and
subduing it. "What's happening at the molecular level is there are two
pieces that can be targeted and that can be grabbed on to," he said.

This new version of vancomycin could be a giant leap forward. [By
binding molecules that bacteria need to build a protective cell wall,
**the drug may help end the antibiotic-bacteria arms race** and
eliminate the need for researchers to continuously design new drugs to
treat newly evolved strains of antibiotic-resistant
bacteria.]{.underline}

Padilla explained that the arms race is an ongoing and expensive
endeavor. "It doesn't really solve the problem," Padilla said. "In terms
of antibiotic development, I believe we shouldn't focus solely on
modifying what we already know works, but rather take a step back and
adopt a new approach."

Padilla and Nowick hope their new family of antibiotics inspires other
researchers to explore similar approaches for treating
antibiotic-resistant pathogens in non-traditional ways.

"What's a new way that we can develop an antibiotic that doesn't require
us to keep doing the same thing over and over again?" said Padilla.  "I
think with our approach, and the approach of several others, [we're
starting to target something that bacteria will most likely not evolve
resistance to."]{.underline}

#### Alternative incentives and funding for AMR has already proven effective\-\--studies prove new biotech is coming now.

**Waldron '25 \[James Waldron; February 24, 2025; EU bureau chief for
Fierce Biotech; worked as an editor specifically in pharma, biotech, and
hospital sectors in the healthcare** industry; "VC firm Kineticos
launches biotech to develop fresh approach to antibiotic resistance",
Fierce Biotech;
https://www.fiercebiotech.com/biotech/vc-firm-kineticos-launches-biotech-develop-fresh-approach-antibiotic-resistance?form=MG0AV3&form=MG0AV3;
accessed 03-04-2025**\] NT-LY**

Venture capital firm [Kineticos **[Life Sciences has launched]{.mark}**
a new [**biotech**]{.mark} to take forward a class of antibiotics forged
in the labs of Harvard University.]{.underline}

[[Kineticos has already set up a]{.mark}n accelerator [fund]{.mark},
dubbed KAMRA I, [to finance]{.mark} life sciences **[companies working
to combat]{.mark}** the growing global threat of [antimicrobial
resistance]{.mark}]{.underline}. Some of this funding has gone toward

oxepanoprolinamides [[that are]{.mark} being touted as [offering highly
effective]{.mark} and differentiated [binding to the bacterial
ribosome]{.mark}]{.underline}.

Oxepanoprolinamides are [[a class of]{.mark} lincosamide
[antibiotics]{.mark} the origins of which lie]{.underline} in the lab of

their [**effectiveness** against]{.mark} both gram-positive and

according to Kinvard Bio's Feb. 24 release.]{.underline}

This research team previously received \$1.2 million from the Combating
Antibiotic-Resistant Bacteria Biopharmaceutical Accelerator to evaluate

Kinvard Bio has now secured the license to this oxepanoprolinamide tech
and is working toward taking potential therapies into the clinic. The

complicated urinary tract infections, community-acquired bacterial

mycobacterial lung disease.

"[The powerful synthetic [methods]{.mark} we\'ve [developed]{.mark} at
the Myers Lab [have enabled]{.mark} us to create [a series of
compounds]{.mark} that hold great promise [**for treating**
superbugs]{.mark} resistant to standard treatments]{.underline}," Myers
said in this morning's release. "Licensing these compounds and the
synthetic platform for further development should accelerate the
production of essential antibiotics to address the pressing global
health issues caused by drug-resistant infections."

Kinvard Bio is headed up by Lloyd Payne, Ph.D., who was previously CEO
of antibiotic company ArrePath and currently is a venture partner for
the KAMRA I fund.

**Bayer '23** \[Bayer; August 10, 2023; global life sciences company

The world urgently needs new antibiotics, but there are few in the

[[there is currently no viable market for]{.mark} new
[antibiotics]{.mark}. New [antibiotics are used sparingly to preserve

is a huge public health need for new antibiotics, but a lack of funding
available for antibiotic R&D, particularly the later stages of clinical
research. This creates a "valley of death" between discovery and patient
access.

## AT Environment Disad

### 2AC\-\--BioD Loss Now

#### US destroying marine life now

David **Shiffman**, **4-23**-25 -- Research Associate in Marine Biology
at Arizona State University. "Trump is stripping protections from marine
protected areas -- why that's a problem for fishing's future, and for
whales, corals and other ocean life," The Conversation U.S.,
https://theconversation.com/trump-is-stripping-protections-from-marine-protected-areas-why-thats-a-problem-for-fishings-future-and-for-whales-corals-and-other-ocean-life-254925.

It's also why countries around the world have been creating [marine
protected areas]{.mark}.

These protected areas, covering over 11.6 million square miles (30
million square kilometers) in 16,000 locations, offer refuge away from
human activities for a wide variety of living creatures, from corals to
sea turtles and whales. [They [give fish stocks]{.mark} a [place to
thrive]{.mark}, and those fish [spread]{.mark} out [into]{.mark} the
[surrounding waters]{.mark}, which [helps fishing industries and local
economies]{.mark}]{.underline}.

In the U.S., [however]{.mark}, **[marine [protection]{.mark} is [being
dismantled by]{.mark} President Donald [Trump]{.mark}]{.underline}**.

Trump [issued]{.mark} a proclamation on [April 17]{.mark}, 2025, titled
"Unleashing American commercial fishing in the Pacific," ordering [the
[removal of key protections]{.mark} to [allow commercial fishing]{.mark}
[in]{.mark} parts of a nearly-500,000-square-mile marine protected area
called the Pacific Island Heritage [National Marine
Monument]{.mark}]{.underline}.

He also [called]{.mark} for a review of [all other marine national
monuments to decide]{.mark} if they should be [open]{.mark}ed [to
commercial fishing]{.mark} too. In addition, the Trump
[[administration]{.mark} is [proposing to redefine "harm" under]{.mark}
the [Endangered Species Act]{.mark} in a way that would [allow for
**more damage**]{.mark} [to]{.mark} these species'
[habitats]{.mark}]{.underline}.

I'm a marine biologist and scuba diver, and it's no accident that all my
favorite dive sites are within marine protected areas. I've found what
scientific studies from across the world show: **[[Protected]{.mark}
[areas have much healthier marine]{.mark} life [populations and
healthier ecosystems]{.mark}.]{.underline}**

What's at risk in the Pacific

The Pacific Island Heritage National [Marine Monument]{.mark}, about 750
miles west of Hawaii, is dotted by coral reefs and atolls, with species
of fish, marine mammals and birds rarely found anywhere else.

It is [[home to protected and endangered species]{.underline}]{.mark},
including turtles, whales and Hawaiian monk seals. Palmyra Atoll and
Kingman Reef, both within the area, are considered [among]{.mark} the
[most]{.mark} [pristine coral reefs]{.mark} in the world, each
[providing]{.mark} [habitats]{.mark} [for]{.mark} a [wide range
of]{.mark} fish and other [species]{.mark}.

These marine species are able to thrive there and spread out into the
surrounding waters because their habitats have been protected.

President George W. Bush, a conservative Republican, created this
protected area in 2009, restricting fishing there, and President Barack
Obama later expanded it. [[Trump]{.mark}, whose administration has made
no secret of its aim to **[strip away environmental
protections]{.mark}** across the [country's land and waters]{.mark}, is
now reopening much of the marine protected area to industrial-scale
fishing.]{.underline}

The risks from industrial fishing

When too many fish are killed and too few young fish are left to replace
them, it's considered [[overfishing]{.mark}, and this has become a
[growing problem]{.mark} around the world]{.underline}.

In 1974, about 10% of the world's fish stocks were overfished. By 2021,
that number had risen to 37.7%, according to the United Nations Food and
Agriculture Organization's annual State of Fisheries and Aquaculture
Report.

Modern industrial-scale fishing practices can also [harm other
species]{.mark}.

Bycatch, or catching animals that fishermen don't want but are
inadvertently caught up in nets and other gear, is a threat to many
endangered species. Many seabirds, sea turtles and whales die this way
each year. Some types of [fishing gear]{.mark}, such as trawls and
dredges that drag along the sea floor to scoop up sea life, can
[**[destroy ocean habitat]{.underline}**]{.mark} itself.

### 1AR\-\--BioD Loss Now

#### 1 million species are at risk.

**WHO**, **2-18**-25 **--** United Nations agency that direct and
coordinate responses to health emergencies. "Biodiversity," World Health
Organization,
https://www.who.int/news-room/fact-sheets/detail/biodiversity.

Healthy communities are sustained by well-functioning ecosystems, which
provide critical services such as clean air, fresh water, natural
medicines and food security. These [[ecosystems]{.mark} also
[regulate]{.mark} [diseases and]{.mark} help [stabilize]{.mark} the
[climate]{.mark}]{.underline}. For example, forests absorb over 2.6
billion tonnes of CO2 annually, contributing to climate regulation and
reducing the incidence of diseases linked to pollution. However,
[[biodiversity loss]{.mark} is [accelerating at]{.mark} an
[unprecedented rate]{.mark}, with approximately [**1 million species**
at risk]{.mark} of extinction]{.underline}, threatening these vital
services and exacerbating public health risks globally.

Impact

People depend on biodiversity in many ways. Human health relies on
ecosystem resources, products and services (such as fresh water, food
and fuel sources; the regulation of crop pests and diseases; and the
regulation of air, water and soil quality) which are needed for good
health and productive livelihoods. Biodiversity loss can [have
[significant]{.mark} direct health [impacts if ecosystem]{.mark}
[services no longer meet societal needs]{.mark}]{.underline}. Changes in
ecosystems can **[[affect livelihoods, income, local migration]{.mark}
and may even [cause or increase political
conflict]{.mark}]{.underline}**.

Significant medical and pharmacological discoveries are made through
greater understanding of the Earth\'s biodiversity. Biological diversity
of [[microorganisms, flora and fauna provides]{.mark} [extensive]{.mark}
[benefits]{.mark} for biological, health, and pharmacological
sciences.]{.underline} It is also the [source of]{.mark} traditional and
complementary [medicines]{.mark}.

Biodiversity loss also [has [profound economic
consequences]{.mark}]{.underline}, particularly in sectors like
agriculture, fisheries and healthcare. It is [estimated]{.mark} that
[the global economic [impact]{.mark} of biodiversity loss [amounts to
**US\$ 10 trillion annually**]{.mark},]{.underline} including healthcare
costs from increased disease transmission and agricultural losses from
pollinator declines. For example, the [[decline in bee
populations]{.mark}, which are responsible for pollinating crops [worth
over US\$ 235 billion annually]{.mark}, [threatens global food security
and nutrition]{.mark}]{.underline}.

Threats to biodiversity and health

Biodiversity loss is occurring at an alarming rate, with recent
estimates showing that [species]{.mark} [extinctions]{.mark} are
currently [**[10 to 100 times]{.underline}** higher than the natural
baseline]{.mark}. This is largely due to human activities like
deforestation, habitat fragmentation, and climate change. This loss
**[[threatens essential ecosystem services]{.mark}, including
pollination, soil fertility, and water purification, with direct
consequences for human health]{.underline}**. For example, the
degradation of wetlands, which filter freshwater, has led to a 35%
decline in global wetland coverage since 1970, [[increasing]{.mark}
waterborne [diseases]{.mark} [and reducing water]{.mark} availability
[for over 2 billion people]{.mark}.]{.underline}

Biodiversity loss and ecosystem degradation are becoming [major health
concerns]{.mark}. When ecosystems are disrupted, services like clean
air, water, and food can be affected. Additionally, we [[lose valuable
natural resources]{.underline}]{.mark} -- like plants [and]{.mark}
animals -- that may hold untapped [benefits for health and
medicine]{.mark}.

### 2AC\-\--Warming Turns BioD Loss

#### Climate change accelerates BioD loss.

**Shivanna 22** (K R Shivanna \[Writer for the Ashoka Trust for Research
in Ecology and the Environment\], "Climate change and its impact on
biodiversity and human welfare", 2022 May 2,
https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC9058818///phs-DZ)

Amongst the [[marine species]{.mark}, corals are the [most affected
groups due to the rise in temperature and acidity of oceans]{.mark}.
[Corals]{.mark} live in a symbiotic relationship with algae which
provide colour and photosynthates to the corals. Corals [are extremely
sensitive to heat and acidity]{.mark}; [even an increase of 2--3°F of
ocean water above normal results in expulsion]{.mark} of the symbiotic
algae from their tissues [leading to]{.mark} their
[bleaching]{.mark}]{.underline} (Hoegh-Guldberg et al. 2017). When this
bleached condition continues for several weeks, corals die. [Nearly
[one-third of the Great Barrier Reef,]{.mark} the world's largest coral
reef system that sustains huge Australian tourism industry, [has died as
a result of global warming]{.mark}]{.underline} (Hughes et al. 2018).
According to the experts the reef will be unrecognizable in another
50 years if greenhouse gas emissions continue at the current rate.

According to UNESCO, coral reefs in all 29 reef-containing [[World
Heritage sites would **cease to exist** as functioning
ecosystems]{.mark} by the end of this century [if
g]{.mark}reen[h]{.mark}ouse [g]{.mark}as [emissions]{.mark} [**continue
to be emitted** at the present rate]{.mark}]{.underline} (Elena et
al. 2020). Recent assessment of the risk of ecosystem collapse to coral
reefs of the Western Indian Ocean, covering about 5% of the global
total, range from critically endangered to vulnerable (Obura et
al. 2021). [[Coral reefs provide suitable habitat for **thousands** of
other species]{.mark}, including sharks, turtles and whales. [If]{.mark}
[corals die, the **whole** ecosystem will get
disrupted]{.mark}.]{.underline}

[M[elting of ice in Arctic region due to global warming is
**threatening** the survival of native animals]{.mark} such as polar
bear, Arctic fox and Arctic wolf. [Rising of sea level]{.mark} also
[leads to]{.mark} the [**extinction** of a large number of

Over [180,000 [islands]{.mark} around the globe [contain 20% of]{.mark}

[protected threatened and [endangered species in]{.mark} 23 [coastal

Recently more than 100 Aquatic Science Societies representing over
