(Bonar 2021). [They have highlighted the effects of [climate change on
marine and aquatic ecosystems]{.mark} and [have called on]{.mark} the
[world leaders and public to undertake **mitigation** measures]{.mark}
to protect and sustain aquatic systems and theirs services]{.underline}.

### 1AR\-\--Warming Turns BioD

#### Warming [destroys]{.underline} biodiversity. Prefer [empirical]{.underline} evidence. 

**Habibullah et al 21** \[Muzafar Shah Habibullah, Badariah Haji Din,
Siow-Hooi Tan, and Hasan Zahid, PhD, Professor at the Putra Business
School and a Senior Economist at the Centre for Future Labour Market
Studies 8-2-2021, \"Impact of Climate Change on Biodiversity Loss:
Global Evidence,\" Springer Nature Link,
https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s11356-021-15702-8\] //adiiiiG

[The present [study investigates]{.mark} the [impact of climate change
on biod]{.mark}iversity loss **[using global data]{.mark} consisting of
115 countries**]{.underline}. In this study, [we [measure
biod]{.mark}iversity [loss using data on]{.mark} the total number of
[threatened species]{.mark} of **amphibians**, **birds**, **fishes**,
**mammals**, **mollusks**, **plants**, [and]{.mark}
**reptiles**.]{.underline} The data were compiled from the Red List
published by the International Union for Conservation of Nature (IUCN).
For climate change variables, we have included [[temperature]{.mark},
[precipitation]{.mark}, [and]{.mark} the number of [natural
disaster]{.mark} occurrences]{.underline}. As for the control variable,
we have considered governance indicator and the level of economic
development. By employing ordinary least square with robust standard
error and robust regression (M-estimation), [our [results suggest
that]{.mark} all three [climate change variables]{.mark} -- temperature,
precipitation, and the number of natural disasters occurrences --
**[increase biodiversity loss]{.mark}**.]{.underline} Higher economic
development also impacted biodiversity loss positively. On the other
hand, good governance such as the control of corruption, regulatory
quality, and rule of law reduces biodiversity loss. Thus, practicing
good governance, promoting conservation of the environment, and the
control of greenhouse gasses would able to mitigate biodiversity loss.

Introduction

Biological diversity or its common abbreviation,
[biodiversity,]{.underline} has been [defined as "the totality (numbers)
and variability (types) of living organisms in the ecosystem, region and
the environment"]{.underline} (Butler 2006). The diversity and the
interrelationships within species, between species, and of ecosystem are
fundamental for human health and well-being (GRI 2007; UNEP 2002).
[[Biod]{.mark}iversity provides mankind in a variety of ways --
economically, environmentally, and culturally]{.underline} (GRI 2007;
UNEP 2002; European Commission 2008). Economically, [biodiversity
[provides econ]{.mark}omic [benefits to industries that depend]{.mark}
[on natural resources]{.mark}]{.underline}, [a nation's economic growth
in particular countries depending on oil and gas explorations, source of
food, shelters, fresh water, wood and fiber, fuel, and clean air to the
population.]{.underline} [Biodiversity [subscribes to]{.mark} various
[environmental services such as regulating]{.mark} the [climate]{.mark},
control of [pollution]{.mark} and [soil erosion]{.mark}, ecological
balance, environmental conservation, support species diversity, and
[pollination]{.mark} of various crops]{.underline}. In terms of culture,
"biodiversity has an intrinsic value. [Its support our cultural
identity, offers spiritual inspiration and solace and plays an important
role in our mental and physical well-being"]{.underline} (European
Commission 2008: pp. 4). It also provides educational values, esthetic
and medicinal values, ethical values, and recreational values for the
development of ecotourism and conservation.

The importance of biodiversity has caught the world's attention. In
1992, the leaders of 150 countries signed the "Convention on Biological
Diversity (CBD)" during the Rio Earth Summit, with the commitment to the
biodiversity conservation, biological sustainable resources, and
equitable sharing of benefits from biodiversity (Earthwatch
Institute 2002). However, for almost 3 decades, this effort has not been
a success. In fact, none of the 20 Aichi biodiversity targets has been
fully achieved globally (CBD, 2020). The World Wildlife Fund (WWF 2020)
portrays an alarming picture of the global biodiversity. According to
the Living Planet Index 2020, between 1970 and 2016, [the [population
sizes]{.mark} of amphibians, birds, fish, mammals, and reptiles [have
decreased]{.mark} an average of [68%]{.mark}]{.underline} as forecasted
earlier by WWF (2016).

Figure 1 presents an increasing trend in the number of threatened
species worldwide. [The [number of threatened species]{.mark} -- plants,
fishes, amphibians, mollusks, birds, mammals, insects, and reptiles --
has [shown an **increasing trend**]{.mark} for the last two
decades]{.underline}. For example, for the 10-year period from 2006 to
2015, fishes, mollusks, and reptiles experienced biodiversity loss at an
annual average rate of 8.0%, 8.5%, and 12.5%, respectively. According to
WWF (2016), "[the declined in species is [due to a **variety of
factors** including]{.mark} **unsustainable agriculture**,
**fisheries**, **[mining]{.mark}**, [**habitat** **loss**]{.mark} and
**[degradation]{.mark}**, **[overexploitation]{.mark}**, [**climate**
**change**]{.mark} and **[pollution]{.mark}**]{.underline}". On the
other hand, Millennium Ecosystem Assessment (MEA 2005) reported that
[60% of the ecosystem has been degraded or used
unsustainably]{.underline}. It was found that the [world's ecosystem
changed more rapidly in the second half of the twentieth century than at
any time in human history. Over the past few hundred years, [humans have
increased]{.mark} the [species extinction]{.mark} rate [by]{.mark} as
much as [1000 times]{.mark} over the planet's history.]{.underline}

Numerous studies have reiterated that **[one of the most important
drivers of the current loss of biodiversity is climate
change]{.underline}** (MEA 2005; Brook et al. 2008; Guo et al. 2017).
The rise of temperature and carbon dioxide (CO2) levels has been
associated with global climate changes. These effects give rise to
several potentially major changes in hydrologic cycles (precipitation
and evaporation) and an [[increasin]{.mark}g magnitude and [frequency of
extreme weather]{.mark} such as floods, cyclones, and droughts that will
[have a profound negative impact on biod]{.mark}iversity]{.underline}
(Adler et al. 2009; Rinawati et al. 2013). According to Tol (2009),
climate change is the mother of all externalities and has a profound
impact on biodiversity not only through changes in temperature and
precipitation, but also the ways [climate change [might affect
ocean]{.mark} acidification, [land]{.mark} use and nutrients,
[and]{.mark} also the proliferation of invasive alien species into new
[habitats]{.mark}]{.underline}[.]{.mark} On the other hand, Thomas et
al. (2004) point out that [climate change [could result in]{.mark} the
[extinction of]{.mark} more than [a **million terrestrial
species**]{.mark} in the next 50 years.]{.underline}

The purpose of the present study is to investigate the effects of
climate change on biodiversity loss using worldwide data on the total
number of threatened species (proxy for biodiversity loss). The
CBD (2019) defines biodiversity loss as the loss of the components of
biodiversity that includes biomes, habitats, and ecosystem; species and
populations; and genetic diversity (CBD COP 7 Decision VII/30). In other
words, biodiversity loss is a reduction in the variety of life on Earth
(Chivian and Bernstein 2010). Biodiversity loss in this study is proxy
by the number of threatened species, namely, amphibians, birds, fishes,
mammals, mollusks, plants, and reptiles. On the other hand, to proxy for
the climate change variables, we have included the number of natural
disasters occurrences, temperature, and precipitation. As for the
control variable, we have included real gross domestic product (GDP)
(proxy for the level of economic development) and governance indicator.
Our study is different from the previous works in three aspects: (1) we
have included three measures of climate change variables and hypothesize
to impact biodiversity loss; (2) we have included amphibians, birds,
fishes, mammals, mollusks, plants, and reptiles as proxy for
biodiversity loss; and (3) we employ robust regression approach (the
M-estimator) in our analysis.

Literature review

Climate change is the term used to describe a gradual increase in the
average temperature of the Earth's atmosphere and its ocean. Due to
global warming, the species that live in the forest will be affected,
both by changing habitat and in direct response to temperature increases
and changes in precipitation and extreme weather events (Sarkar 2012).
During the 1990s, climate change emerged as one of the potential threats
to biodiversity (UNEP 2002). Furthermore, climate change could lead to
severe adverse impacts on ecosystems and on the goods and services they
provide (IPCC 2001). Climate change is an umbrella crisis in that it has
social, economic, political, and ecological impacts, including
biodiversity loss (Rosales 2008). Evidences have shown that climate
change can affect biodiversity among other things, by changing life
cycles and developing new physical traits and by shifting habitat ranges
and species distribution, changes in abundance, changes in migration
patterns, and changes in the frequency and severity of pest and disease
outbreaks (Reed 2012; Hui 2013; Sintayehu 2018).

Empirical studies that have been conducted to investigate the impact of
climate change on the loss of biodiversity are rather lacking. For
example, earlier work by Asafu-Adjaye (2003) by using dummy variable to
account for climatic effects on biodiversity found that climate change
has positive impact of the number of threatened mammals, birds, and
plants. Similar work by Shandra et al. (2010) by using dummy variable to
measure climate change, however, found that tropical climate has no
effects on the number of threatened species -- mammals and birds, but
when using forest loss as proxy for biodiversity loss, Shandra et al.
(2011) found evidence that tropical climate affected forest loss or
deforestation.

On the other hand, Gren et al. (2016) have investigated the effects of
climate change on biodiversity loss at the global scale by using both
temperature and precipitation to proxy for climate change. The effects
of climate change such as the increased in temperature and precipitation
affect individual organisms, populations, species distribution, and
ecosystem compositions and functions (UNU-IAS 2010; Reed 2012).
Similarly, Amano et al. (2018) also include both temperature and
precipitation in their study on global waterbird populations. Findings
from both studies show positive relationships between biodiversity loss
and both temperature and precipitation. [The increase in temperature and
precipitation increases the loss of biodiversity.]{.underline}
Villen-Perez (2020) also found that [climate change has affected plant
species in Finland]{.underline}. The temperature-sensitive species in
all functional groups from dwarf shrubs, herbs, and grasses to
bryophytes and lichens migrate northwards to cooler climate.

Nevertheless, [the effects of climate change through **extreme weather
events** such as floods, cyclones, and droughts also have **profound
negative impacts** on biodiversity]{.underline}. Thus, the [occurrences
of natural disaster would have a positive impact on biodiversity
loss]{.underline}. [[Natural disasters]{.mark} could be the results of
**earthquakes**, **extreme** **temperature**, volcanic eruptions,
**[landslides]{.mark}**, **[storms]{.mark}**, **[wildfire]{.mark}**,
**[flood]{.mark}**, and **[drought]{.mark}**.]{.underline} A country
that frequently experiences one or more of these catastrophic events
will have a devastating impact on biodiversity. Wagstaff (2017) pointed
out that **[[deforestation]{.underline}]{.mark}**, [increased in the
frequency of wildfires, severity of tropical storms, and extreme
floodings]{.underline} (see also Chapin and Diaz 2020) [have tragically
[resulted in]{.mark} the [loss of biod]{.mark}iversity.]{.underline}

Studies by Miura and Kanaya (2017), Porwal et al. (2012), and Fattorini
et al. (2018) on earthquake; Bixby et al. (2015) on wild fire; Milner et
al. (2013) on flood; Kwit et al. (2000) on hurricane; Fraver (2017) on
tornado; and Darnaedi and Zulkarnaen (2017) and Lallement et al. (2016)
on volcanic eruptions clearly indicated the devastating impact of these
natural events on the ecosystem, habitat, and biotic. For example, [the
2011 Japan earthquake resulted in the disappearance of vegetation and
altered habitat structure through changes in topography, bottom
elevation, and sediment characteristics which induced drastic changes in
associated biotic communities]{.underline} (Kanaya et al. 2017; Miura
and Kanaya 2017). [Natural disasters]{.underline} such as earthquakes,
landslides, floods, droughts, forest fires, extreme low temperature, and
freezing rain have [led to the destruction of many species in
China]{.underline} (Guan 2017). In Indonesia, Darnaedi and Zulkarnaen
(2017) reported that the [Krakatau volcanic eruption has affected
biodiversity of the islands around Krakatau]{.underline}, the western
part of Java, and the southeastern part of Sumatra. On the other hand,
in India, the December 2004 tsunami has impacted the archipelago of
Andaman and Nicobar Islands. The island suffered great loss of forest
and coastal biodiversity, in which the mangroves, littoral forest, beach
forest, and low land swamp were most affected (Porwal et al. 2012).

Methodology

To estimate the impact of climate change on biodiversity loss, following
the work of others (Asafu-Adjaye 2003; Shandra et al. 2010; Gren et
al. 2016), we specify the following augmented biodiversity loss-climate
change equation. In this study, we have included the number of natural
disaster occurrences, temperature, and precipitation to proxy for
climate change:

where the parameters  are to be estimated and ϵi indicates the error
term. The error term is assumed to well behave with mean zero and
constant variance. It is a priori that we expect α1, α2, α3, and α4\> 0
and α5\<0. Thus, there is a positive relationship between climate change
(natural disaster, temperature, precipitation) and biodiversity loss, in
which increase in the frequency of natural disaster events, temperature,
and precipitation will lead to an increase in the loss of biodiversity,
and on the other hand, the control variables such as wealth of the
nation (realGDP) will impact biodiversity loss positively, while good
governance (governance) will affect biodiversity loss negatively. The
subscript "i" refers to country. In this study, we employ
cross-sectional data analysis for 115 countries. The list of countries
included in the study is presented in Table 1.

For the dependent variable, we follow the works of Naidoo and Adamowicz
(2001), Asafu-Adjaye (2003), Hoffmann (2004), Shandra et al. (2010), and
Halkos (2011) to proxy biodiversity loss by using the number of
threatened species at the country level. In this study, we summed all
the critically endangered and vulnerable categories of amphibians,
birds, fishes, mammals, mollusks, plants, and reptiles. This
classification was given by the International Union for the Conservation
of Nature and Natural Resources (IUCN) Red List of Threatened Animals.
According to Hoffmann (2004), the Red List is a comprehensive listing of
threatened and endangered species in most nations and the most widely
used source on threatened and endangered species (see also Ryden et
al. 2020; Bennun et al. 2018; Gren et al. 2016).

As for the control variable, we use real GDP to proxy for the wealth of
a nation or level of economic development. GDP can be a good indicator
to operationalize "treadmill of production theory" of the impact of
economic growth on environmental degradation (York et al. 2003;
Schnaiberg and Gould 1994). For a country to strive for higher level of
economic development, it requires that higher level of economic output
to be produced. It is predicted that higher GDP may stimulate demand for
agricultural and forest-derived product and may cause deforestation,
thus increasing the number of threatened species (Asafu-Adjaye 2003;
Culas 2007; Shandra et al. 2010). Furthermore, Rosales (2008) reiterates
that "economic growth is a prime catalyst of biodiversity loss because
people desire economic growth for dissimilar reasons -- some for the
increased accumulation of wealth, others for basic needs". Rosales
further notes that because economic growth is the increase of production
and consumption of goods and services, these goods and services derive
from the ecosystem that we live; thus, the impact of economic growth on
the natural world increases with the size of the economy, and achieving
higher economic growth comes with a price. For example, according to Suh
(2006), in the USA, a dollar's worth of goods or services produced, on
average, 0.36 kg of CO2 goes into the atmosphere. Thus, economic growth
is said to be the main driver in increased GHG emissions. Furthermore,
Wilting et al. (2017) reiterated that the GHG emissions associated with
the production and consumption of goods and services will result in
biodiversity loss.

On the other hand, Eagles (2009) asserts that conservation of diversity
can only be attained through good governance and good management. In
fact, Baynham-Herd et al. (2018) posited that governance has been shown
to be an important predictor of biodiversity loss. For example, a poorly
governed country or a corrupt government is less likely to invest in
infrastructure for conservation; willing to allow poaching, illegal
logging, and other illegal activities; and lower enforcement of
environmental laws that can act as incentive for overexploitation of
resources (Ferraro 2005; Eklund et al. 2011), ultimately resulting in
environmental degradation and biodiversity loss.

Study that link governance and the loss of biodiversity was conducted by
Smith et al. (2003). In their study, the governance indicator was based
on the Corruption Perception Index produced by the Transparency
International and also the International Country Risk Guide, while
biodiversity loss was proxy by using changes in forest cover and African
elephant and black rhinoceros populations. They found strong negative
relationship between governance scores and changes in the number of
African elephants and black rhinoceroses and in forest cover. On the
contrary, similar study by Barrett et al. (2006) found that governance
has no effects on biodiversity loss, after taking into account other
control variables such as population density, human development index,
and per capita GDP. Nevertheless, recent studies reinforce the link
between good governance and the loss of biodiversity. For example,
Melick et al. (2012) posited that poor governance and lack of political
will on the part of the government of Papua New Guinea that led to most
of the protected areas ineffective. Lack of government support and lack
of funding and resources retard conservation efforts.

In another study, Amano et al. (2018) investigated the impact of
effective governance on the global waterbird populations. They found
that waterbirds are abundance in countries that are having effective
governance. In countries or regions of western and central Asia,
sub-Saharan Africa, and South America, where governance is on average
less effective, the number of waterbird declines. According to Amano et
al. (2018), ineffective governance is associated with the absence of
positive attitudes to environmental protection, weakly enforced
environmental legislation, low levels of investment in conservation,
ineffective hunting regulations, and political instability resulted in
habitat loss and degradation. On the other hand, a study by Baynham-Herd
et al. (2018) also suggested the importance of governance in mitigating
biodiversity loss. They found that governance was an important predictor
of responses to biodiversity conservation than economic wealth. This
result corroborates with earlier findings by McClanahan and Rankin
(2016) and Rodrigues et al. (2014) that wealth is unrelated to
protecting threatened species.

Empirical results

In this study, [we have [compiled]{.mark} a **cross-national of [115
countries]{.mark}** [using data]{.mark} for the year 2011 and 2016 for
the analysis.]{.underline} Data for the number of threatened species
were compiled from the Red List published by IUCN. Data for the number
of natural disaster occurrences was compiled from the Office of Foreign
Disaster Assistance (OFDA)/Centre for Research on the Epidemiology of
Disaster (CRED), while data on temperature and precipitation were taken
from the World Bank database on temperature and precipitation which is
available at https://climateknowledgeportal.worldbank.org/download-data.
On the other hand, data for real GDP were compiled from the World
Development Indicators available at the World Bank database. It is
accessible at https://data.worldbank.org/indicator, while data on
governance indicators were collected from the Worldwide Governance
Indicators (WGI) at http://info.worldbank.org/governance/wgi/. Worldwide
Governance Indicators published six governance indicators, namely,
control of corruption, government effectiveness, political stability and
absence of violent, regulatory quality, rule of law, and voice and
accountability (see Kaufman et al. 2008).

Table 2 provides the descriptive statistics for the dependent and
independent variables in our analysis. More importantly, it shows that,
in general, most dependent and independent variables demonstrate
substantial standard deviations, skewness, and kurtosis. In all cases,
we have positive value of skewness (except temperature and political
stability for having negative skewness) for all variables, while
kurtosis with size greater than 3 was shown by all dependent variables
and real GDP. These indicate that the series show longer or fatter tail
on the right side of the distribution. In other words, the kurtosis
shows a leptokurtic type of distribution for all the threatened species
as well as for real GDP. Nevertheless, the Jarque-Bera test for
normality of the series was rejected for all series except for political
stability. In view that many of the variables involved in our analysis
demonstrate extreme skewness and kurtosis and have non-normal
distribution; the common method to circumvent these problems is to
transform all variables into logarithm (McKinney et al. 2009; Naidoo and
Adamowicz 2001; Ehrhardt-Martinez et al. 2002). In this study, we employ
the formula  to transform the series into logarithm (Busse and
Hefeker 2007). By employing this method, we maintain the sign of yt.

In Table 3, we present the correlation matrix for the dependent
variables and all the independent variables. Generally, [the [climate
change variables]{.mark}]{.underline} -- [natural disaster occurrence,
temperature, and precipitation]{.underline} -- [[show **positive
association** with all threatened species]{.underline}]{.mark} except
for mollusks. Similarly, real GDP shows positive association with all
threatened species, while the six governance indicators exhibit negative
association with all the threatened species except for mollusks.
Interestingly, none of the independent variables demonstrates potential
multicollinearity among themselves. The correlation coefficients between
all independent variables are no greater than 0.7. However, as expected,
the governance indicators suggest multicollinearity among the six
governance measures, with association higher than 0.7. Taking this into
consideration, we have estimated all equations for the seven threatened
species by incorporating the six governance indicators one at a time.

Despite similar work on cross-national data analysis, however, unlike
the works of Benhin and Barbier (2004), Shandra (2007), McKinney et al.
(2009), and Shandra et al. (2011, 2016) which have used ordinary least
squares (OLS) to estimate Eq. (1) above, instead in this study, we
follow Asafu-Adjaye (2003) and Barrett et al. (2006) by employing OLS
with robust standard errors, to correct for heteroskedasticity that may
be present in the data. In order to do this, we have estimated Eq. (1)
by using OLS with White's autocorrelation-heteroskedasticity-consistent
standard errors estimator (Newey and West 1987).

The results of our analyses are presented in Table 4 for 2011. Some
explanations are needed here. First, initially all regression equations
are estimated using OLS, and the diagnostics tests such as
Breush-Pagan-Godfrey (BPG), Glejser and White tests for
heteroscedasticity, Ramsey Reset test for misspecification, and
Jarque-Bera test for normality of the residuals are reported in
Table 4 for reference. The diagnostic tests clearly suggest that in some
cases the assumption of OLS was violated. Taking this into
consideration, we have estimated all regressions by employing OLS with
robust standard error due to Newey-West procedure, and these results are
reported in Table 4. And secondly, the estimated regressions presented
in Table 4 are the final estimated models for each threatened species
with different measures of governance indicator. In this study, we have
selected the control of corruption as proxy for governance measure for
birds, mammals, and reptiles; rule of law for amphibians, fishes, and
mollusks; and regulatory quality for plants. Each of this governance
measure was chosen among the six measures based on three model selection
criteria such as the Akaike (AIC), Schwarz (SC), and Hannan-Quinn (HQ)
information criterion. Estimated regression that gives the smallest
value for AIC, SC, and HQ will be chosen as the best fitting model.
Results for OLS and all six governance indicators are available from the
authors upon request.

Our main purpose of conducting this study is to determine the effects of
climate change on biodiversity loss measured by the number of threatened
species. [Climate change was measured by the number of natural disaster
occurrences, temperature, and precipitation]{.underline}. As shown in
Table 4, the adjusted R2 shows that the goodness of fit is satisfactory
for a cross-sectional data analysis for all the estimated models. In
Table 4, [our [estimated regression]{.mark} equations [suggest that
climate change plays an **important role** in]{.mark} contributing to
[the **loss of biodiversity**]{.mark} or the increase in the number of
threatened species for cross-sectional countries]{.underline} in 2011.
Generally, all three climate change indicators -- frequency of natural
disaster occurrences, temperature, and precipitation -- are
statistically significant at least at the 5% significance level. A 1%
increase in the number of frequency in natural disaster events increases
biodiversity loss ranging from 0.24 (mammals and mollusks) to 0.52%
(plants). Among these three measures, it seems that rainfall
(precipitation) has greater impact on biodiversity loss, followed by
temperature and natural disasters. For example, [a 1% increase in
rainfall, on average, increases biodiversity loss for plant species by
2.04%]{.underline} compared to a 1.13% increase in the number of
threatened reptiles as a result in the 1% increase in temperature. Our
results are similar to the work done by Amano et al. (2018) on waterbird
populations in which they found that the impact of precipitation is much
larger than the impact of temperature. On the contrary, Gren et al.
(2016) found that the impact of temperature is much greater than the
impact of precipitation on biodiversity loss using global data.
Nevertheless, our study seems to suggest that the impacts of
precipitation and temperature are more destructive on biodiversity loss
compared to the occurrences of natural disaster events.

On the other hand, real GDP that proxy for the level of economic
development clearly suggests that, on average, an increase in economic
prosperity does not reduce biodiversity loss, thus supporting the
treadmill of production theory. The effects of real GDP on biodiversity
loss are positive and statistically significant at 1% level in all
estimated threatened species equations. In other words, achieving higher
economic growth is at the expense of biodiversity. Higher economic
growth will drive for further economic development, urbanization, energy
consumption, and CO2 emissions. Nonetheless, uncontrolled development
will result in environmental degradation and biodiversity loss as
predicted by the treadmill of production theory. Similar findings were
also found by Barrett et al. (2006), Shandra et al. (2016), Clausen and
York (2008), Naidoo and Adamowicz (2001), McKinney et al. (2009), Gren
et al. (2016), and Rosales (2008). Generally, on average, a 1% increase
in real GDP increases the number of threatened species ranging from 0.19
to 0.32%.

Lastly, our estimated regressions suggest that the relationships between
good governance and biodiversity loss are negative. In other words, on
average, our results suggest that the improvement in governance through
low level of corruption practices and better enforcement of regulatory
quality mitigates biodiversity loss. Our results support the earlier
works by Umemiya et al. (2010), Smith et al. (2003), Culas (2007), Amano
et al. (2018), and Baynham-Herd et al. (2018). The studies by Amano et
al. (2018) and Baynham-Herd et al. (2018) suggest that biodiversity
conservation responses positive to good governance. In our case, by
inspecting the magnitude of the estimated parameters that is their
elasticities, the strongest effects of good governance on biodiversity
loss originate from control of corruption, followed by regulatory
quality. For example, on average, a 10% increase in good governance, for
example, by increasing the control of corruption and the number of
threatened species or biodiversity loss, reduces by 4.5%. Similar impact
can be said for the enforcement of rule of law in mitigating
biodiversity loss. Therefore, regulatory quality and control of
corruption are good policy mix that can be used to mitigate the loss of
biodiversity worldwide.

Robustness checks: further analysis

How robust is our results on the effects of climate change on the number
of threatened species presented above? To check on the robustness of the
positive impact of climate change on biodiversity loss, we have endeavor
to (a) extend our data point 5 years ahead to 2016 and (b) employing
robust regression such as using the M-estimator. The OLS robust results
for using 2016 data are presented in Table 5. Generally, on average, the
results indicate that the three climate change indicators show positive
impact on biodiversity loss. For example, the frequency of natural
disaster occurrences positively impacted the number of threatened
amphibians, birds, plants, and reptiles; temperature affects all
threatened species positively except for the number of threatened
mollusks, while precipitation also increases the number of threatened
amphibians, fishes, plants, and reptiles. Similarly, in support of our
earlier findings, increase in the level of economic development
increases the number of all threatened species -- amphibians, birds,
fishes, mammals, mollusks, plants, and reptiles. On the other hand, our
governance indicator -- the control of corruption and regulatory quality
-- shows that biodiversity loss can be mitigated by good governance
practices.

To our second robustness test, we employ the M-estimator as our robust
regression estimation. In Fig. 2, we demonstrate the boxplots of the
residuals of all estimated regressions presented in Tables 4 and 5. The
boxplots give the overall information about the univariate data (the
residuals) distribution. The core data is plotted as a rectangular box,
while the top and bottom are the lower and the higher quartiles of the
data distribution, respectively. The horizontal line in the box denotes
the data median. The lines above and below the box are called the
whiskers. The whiskers have a length equal to 1.5 times the
interquantile range, and the outliers are the objects located above or
below the whiskers (Daszykowski et al. 2007). As shown in Fig. 2, the
residuals of some of the estimated regressions contain outliers. These
outliers are located at the top and/or bottom of the upper and/or lower
whiskers. These are in the cases of birds, fishes, mammals, plants, and
reptiles.

Barnett and Lewis (1994) have stated that the presence of outliers can
lead to inflated error rates and substantial distortions of parameter
and statistical estimates when using either parametric or non-parametric
tests. Statistically, the increase in error variance will reduce the
power of the statistical tests, decrease normality, and seriously bias
or influence parameter estimates (Perez et al. 2013). According to
Rousseeuw (1984), robust regression is the best method to detect
outliers and provides results that are resistant to the outliers. The
most common general method of robust regression is the M-estimation
method introduced by Huber (1964).

Results of the robust regression estimations using the M-estimators are
given in Table 6 for year 2011 and Table 7 for year 2016. Generally,
results in both tables confirm that climate change has a detrimental
impact on the number of threatened species. For instance, the frequent
occurrences of natural disaster positively affected biodiversity loss,
in particular, the number of threatened amphibians, birds, mammals,
plants, and reptiles. Similarly, rising temperature will increase the
number of threatened of all species except for mollusks. On the other
hand, increase in precipitation will also increase biodiversity loss.
For year 2011, precipitation affects positively on the number of
threatened amphibians, fishes, plants, and reptiles, while results for
data 2016 indicate that increase in precipitation will increase the
number of threatened of all species except for mollusks.

Consistent with our earlier results, the impact of an increase in
economic development has adverse effects on biodiversity. Results in
both Tables 6 and 7 clearly indicate that striving for economic growth
inevitably will result in biodiversity loss. Lastly, our robust
regression results suggest that good governance do mitigate biodiversity
loss. The results show that the control of corruption, enforcement of
regulatory quality, and rule of law can restrict activities that are
detrimental to biodiversity.

Conclusion

In this study, [we have estimated the impact of climate change on
biodiversity loss.]{.underline} Using a cross-national sample of 115
countries, [we are able to determine that climate change -- the
occurrences of natural disaster events, temperature, and precipitation,
play important role in affecting biodiversity loss.]{.underline} [Our
study suggests that the impact on biodiversity loss is more affected by
the changes in precipitation and temperature]{.underline} than the
changes in the frequency of the natural disaster events. Nevertheless,
all these effects point to global warming, in which it was the result
when nations strive for higher economic growth. In this sense, Rosales
(2008) was right to infer that "economic growth is the main driver of
climate change-related biodiversity loss".

In this study, we also found that the level of economic development
contributes in the loss of biodiversity. [Thus, for effective
biodiversity conservation, a [nation should]{.mark} use their wealth and
effort to [reduce the amount of carbon dioxide emissions]{.mark} in
order to combat global warming and natural disaster
mishaps.]{.underline} The government and the industry players including
the financial sector should be held responsible for the loss in
biodiversity. Their activities in pursuing the ultimate goal of
prosperity and profits are at the expense of biodiversity which, on the
other hand, is very important to support life on Earth. Nevertheless,
effective conservation efforts without the control of corruption and
enforcement of rule of law will not do much help in mitigating the loss
of biodiversity.

#### Empirically, [fluctuations in climate]{.underline} have, and will continue to, contribute to [biodiversity loss]{.underline}.

**Butler '07** -- Rhett Butler is the founder and CEO of Mongabay, a
nonprofit organization that delivers news and inspiration from Nature\'s
frontline via a global network of reporters. (Rhett Ayers; \"Global
warming may cause biodiversity extinction\"; Mongabay Environmental

https://news.mongabay.com/2007/03/global-warming-may-cause-biodiversity-extinction/;

The impact of global warming on biodiversity extinction

[Extinction is a hotly debated, but poorly understood topic in
science]{.underline}. The same goes for climate change. When scientists
try to forecast the impact of global change on future biodiversity
levels, the results are contentious, to say the least.

While some argue that species have managed to survive worse climate
change in the past and that current threats to biodiversity are
overstated, many biologists say the impacts of climate change and
resulting shifts in rainfall, temperature, sea levels, ecosystem
composition, and food availability will have significant effects on
global species richness.

[Lessons from historic extinctions]{.underline}

[There is [little doubt]{.mark} that [climate]{.mark} has
[played]{.mark} a [critical role in]{.mark} past [fluctuations of
biod]{.mark}iversity levels. [Among]{.mark} the [**five**
recognized]{.mark} **[mass extinction events]{.mark}** --- the
Ordovician, the Devonian, the Permian, the Triassic and the Cretaceous
--- **[at least four]{.mark}** are believed to [have]{.mark} some
[correlation to climate]{.mark} change.]{.underline}

Peter Ward, a paleontologist at the University of Washington in Seattle,
says [there is evidence that **[most mass extinctions]{.mark}** were
[caused by **gradual**]{.mark} **[c]{.mark}**limate **[c]{.mark}**hange.
[Specifically]{.mark} he cites [the **Triassic and Permian
extinctions**]{.mark} of 200 million and 251 million years ago,
respectively.]{.underline}

"The Triassic event isn't something that happened overnight," said Ward,
noting that [carbon dioxide levels in the atmosphere then were up to
**100 times** what they are today.]{.underline}

In the case of the Permian, [rising temperatures may have caused the
greatest mass extinction on record]{.underline}, according to a study
published in the September 2005 issue of Geology. [[Global
warming]{.underline}]{.mark}, which may have produced temperatures 10 to
30 degrees Celsius (18-54 degrees F) higher than today, [is believed to
have [wiped out 95%]{.mark} of [life]{.mark} forms [in]{.mark} the
world's [oceans]{.mark} [and almost **75% of terrestrial
species**]{.mark}.]{.underline}

While climate change has caused species extinction, it has also led to
the birth of new species, including mankind.

[[During]{.mark} the]{.underline} Paleocene-Eocene Thermal Maximum
([[PETM]{.mark}), a period]{.underline} 55 million years ago [[marked
by]{.mark} a [rapid rise in g]{.mark}reen[h]{.mark}ouse
[g]{.mark}ase[s]{.mark} that heated Earth by roughly 9° F (5° C) in less
than 10,000 years]{.underline}, [climate [warming
caused]{.mark}]{.underline} widespread changes, including [[mass
extinction in]{.mark} the world's [oceans from acidification and shifts
of plant]{.mark} communitie[s due to]{.mark} [changes in
rainfall]{.mark}]{.underline}. The era helped set the stage for the "Age
of Mammals," which included the first appearance of modern primates.

Later periods of climate change in Africa may have created conditions
that lef to the evolution of humans. Dr Mark Maslin, Senior Lecturer in
Geography at University College London, agues that climate variation
over the last 2.7 million years played a crucial part in enhancing human
development. His work suggests that humans evolved during short periods
of great environmental change --- when dry periods were punctuated by
large rapidly appearing and disappearing lakes. It was these rapid
changes in water sources that forced primitive hominid communities to
rapidly change and adapt

"These temporary humid periods would have imposed huge impacts on early
humans," he stated at the 2005 Annual Conference of the Royal
Geographical Society in London. "Our research provides strong support
for theories in which early human species evolved and spread out in
response to a rapidly changing environment."

[[Climate]{.mark} change-induced extinction **[breeds
biod]{.mark}iversity**, but with a delay]{.underline}

Despite past extinctions, on a geological time scale Earth presently has
more species than ever before. However, it is important to note that
while climate change has given rise to new species following mass
extinction, the [**[recovery time]{.mark}** is [measured in]{.mark} the
**[millions of years]{.mark}**, whereas [humans]{.mark} have [only been
around for less than 200,000]{.mark} years. Past extinction teaches us
that biodiversity takes a long time to recover and species loss in
coming years will not be replaced on any meaningful time
scale.]{.underline}

Most biologists agree that [[we are]{.mark} presently [in]{.mark} the
[midst of]{.mark} a [sixth great extinction event]{.mark}, one [where
**species loss** is **outpacing**]{.mark} species
**[birth]{.mark}**]{.underline}. In a paper published last year in the
Annual Review of Ecology, Evolution, and Systematics, Camille Parmesan,
a biologist from the University of Texas at Austin, argued that current
global warming has already caused extinctions in the world's most
sensitive habitats and will continue to cause more species to go extinct
over the next 50 to 100 years. Parmesan's work shows that while some
species---especially those with short generation times like
insects---are evolving in response to climate change, they are not
evolving in ways that could prevent extinction. [Overall species
evolution is not making up for species disappearance.]{.underline}

Current extinctions

To date, the most comprehensive forecast for the impact of climate
change on global biodiversity was made in two 2004 papers published in
Nature by Chris Thomas and colleagues. Analyzing the distributions of
1103 species of animals and plants from various parts of the world, the
authors showed that 15-37 percent are likely to go extinct based on the
best projections of future climate change.

"We matched up the current geographical distribution of each of these
1103 species with the climatic conditions in these areas, and then asked
"[how large an area of this type of climate will exist **[in
2050]{.mark}**, and where might it be]{.underline}?" Dr. Thomas, a
biologist from the University of York, explained to mongabay.com via
email. "[[In many cases]{.mark}, the **[climate]{.mark}** currently
[occupied by a species]{.mark} was **[expected to disappear
entirely]{.mark}**; [in other cases]{.mark}, there was [no
geographic]{.mark} [overlap]{.mark} [between where]{.mark} the [species
currently occurs and where]{.mark} the climatic [conditions]{.mark} will
[remain suitable]{.mark} -- the species would have to move or
potentially face extinction."]{.underline}

Dr. Thomas says that the uncertainty association with climate change
projections make biodiversity estimates tricky, but that their
[projections suggest that [climate change could rival]{.mark}
[human]{.mark}-caused [habitat loss as]{.mark} the [**leading driver**
of]{.mark} species [extinction]{.mark}.]{.underline}

"Using a variety of climate scenarios, assumptions and methods of
analysis, we estimated that somewhere between 5% and 50% of the species
we analyzed are at risk of extinction, with the central range of
estimates falling between 15% and 37%. [This is on the basis of warming
projected until 2050, so the year 2100 risks of extinction from climate
change are likely to lie in the upper half of this range (or
above),"]{.underline} he explained. "[Our estimates of potential
extinction were preliminary values intended to define the order of
magnitude of the problem. We found that the extinction risks from
climate change are probably similar to those from habitat loss, and
conceivably even greater in some regions.]{.underline}"

Dr. Thomas cautions that not all the species "committed to extinction"
will disappear by 2050 due to the inherent lag time of extinction.

"When the climate becomes unsuitable for the long-term survival of a
species, it does not mean that it will die out immediately. For species
with long-lived individuals, in particular, it may be many decades or
even centuries before the last individuals die out. So, these are the
numbers of species that may be declining towards extinction from 2050
onwards, not the numbers that will have died out by that date."

How does climate change affect biodiversity?

[[Climate change]{.mark} can [affect species in **myriad
ways**]{.mark}]{.underline} including the [[expansion,
contraction]{.mark}, and "[migration" of habitat]{.mark}]{.underline};
[[increased]{.mark} incidence of [disease]{.mark} and [invasive
species]{.mark}; [changes in]{.mark} temperature, precipitation, and
other [environmental conditions]{.mark}; [shifts in food
availability]{.mark}; and [failure of eco]{.mark}logical [relationships
with other species]{.mark}]{.underline} --- for example the loss of
critical pollinators or mutualistic nutrient fixers. In the past some
species may have escaped extinction by "migrating" north or southward in
response to climate change. [Today [humans]{.mark} have [made]{.mark} it
a lot [tougher]{.mark} [by]{.mark} fragmenting, converting, and
[destroying habitats]{.mark} and potential migration
corridors.]{.underline}

Peter Raven, director of the Missouri Botanical Garden and a renowned
expert on biodiversity, says that climate change will also make
conservation efforts more difficult.

"As the climate changes, [[protected areas]{.mark} will [not]{.mark} be
[able to shift due to]{.mark} surrounding [urban]{.mark} areas [and
ag]{.mark}ricultural [zones]{.mark}]{.underline}," he told mongabay.com
via telephone. "This makes them all the more susceptible to the impact
of climate change, whether it is rising sea levels, a dip in
precipitation levels, or warmer temperatures."

"Habitat loss will interact with climate change too," added Dr. Thomas.
"It is hard enough to conserve enough land to protect the world's
biodiversity if it stays still -- how much harder if it is moving
around, as species shift their distributions into new areas where the
climate becomes suitable for them. One needs to protect where species
are now, where they will have to get to in future, and land in between
that they must traverse on the way. Thus, the first response to maintain
biodiversity in the context of climate change is to renew efforts to
protect large areas of natural and semi-natural habitats, particularly
in mountain ranges and other environmentally diverse regions -- where
species may be able to survive by moving relatively short distances from
lower to higher elevations, from drier to moister soils (and vice
versa), and so on."

If so many species are expected to die out, then where are all the
extinctions?

Earth's biodiversity is still poorly known. Dr. Raven estimates that
less than one-sixth of species have even been named, let alone assessed
for their risk to climate change. As such, most extinction will occur
among species that are small and poorly known. Further, species
extinction is expected to accelerate significantly around mid-century
should climate forecasts prove accurate, says Dr. Thomas.

"[We expect to see most extinction occurring from 2050 onwards, not
yet,"]{.underline} he said. "But there is increasing evidence that
species are declining at the low altitude and low latitude edges of
their geographic ranges. And last year, Alan Pounds and colleagues
published a paper indicating that over 1% of all of the world's
amphibian species (the harlequin frogs) have succumbed to
climate-triggered outbreaks of a fungal skin pathogen. This is both
sooner that I expected, and worse than I feared."

Dr. Thomas is referring to the study which found that [almost two-thirds
of the 110 known [harlequin]{.mark} Atelopus [frog species]{.mark} of
Central and South America [went extinct in]{.mark} the 1980s and
19[90s]{.mark}.]{.underline} [The [leading culprit]{.mark} for the
demise [was]{.mark} a type of [chytrid fungus]{.mark}]{.underline}
(Batrachochytrium dendrobatidis), an infectious skin disease. The
[researchers discovered strong correlations between **changes in
climate** and the last known sightings of the frogs]{.underline}.
According to the scientists, the Earth's rising temperatures enhance
cloud cover on tropical mountains, leading to cooler days and warmer
nights, both of which favor the chytrid fungus which grows and
reproduces best at temperatures between 63 to 77 degrees Fahrenheit (17
to 25 degrees Celsius).

"The [harlequin frog extinctions represent an important
reminder]{.underline}. None of the threats affecting the world's
biodiversity act in isolation. [[C]{.mark}limate [c]{.mark}hange [helped
**trigger**]{.mark} **[epidemics]{.mark}** of an emerging disease, and
it was the **[combined forces]{.mark}** of these two threats that [was
so damaging]{.mark} to the harlequin frogs."]{.underline}

These brightly colored frogs may just be the proverbial "canary in the
coal mine": an increasing number of studies suggest that climate change
could well put a wide variety of plants and animals at risk of
extinction.

Beyond frogs: lemurs, birds, and coral reefs

[Patricia Wright, one of the world's foremost lemur experts, found that
even [small shifts in rainfall]{.mark} patterns can [drastically affect
lemur population]{.mark}. "A slight change in climate, even in the
rainforest where we assume there is plenty of water, can impact infant
survival for lemurs,"]{.underline} she said following the publication of
a PNAS paper in 2005 that showed how even subtle changes in climate
directly result in lower reproductive success in lemurs. "[I was shocked
to see the effect of rainfall decreases of such small magnitude on the
survival of infant sifakas. The [implications]{.mark} are [huge in a
world **full** of **endangered species**]{.mark}." She says that
deforestation and habitat fragmentation will only worsen the
threat.]{.underline}

Similarly, [climate modeling of the tropical forests of Northern [South
America]{.mark}]{.underline}, Dr. Paul A. T. Higgins [[expect]{.mark}s a
regional [decline in biod]{.mark}iversity [due to low]{.mark}er levels
of [rainfall]{.mark}. He says [habitat destruction]{.mark} will
[make]{.mark} it [more difficult]{.mark} for species to persist in
shifting forests.]{.underline}

Meanwhile, research on birds indicated that up to 72 percent of bird
species in northeastern Australia and more than a third in Europe could
go extinct due to global warming. The WWF report, which reviewed more
than 200 scientific articles on birds found that species most at risk
included migratory, mountain, island, wetland, Arctic, Antarctic and
seabirds. Last year, Britain's environmental protection agency (DEFRA)
warned that climate change will disrupt breeding, hamper migrations, and
increase disease transmission in migratory birds and animals.

Finally, a series of studies published over the past few years have
expressed dire concern for coral reefs[. Among the most alarming was one
by WWF and the Queensland government that said [Australia's Great
Barrier Reef]{.mark} could [lose **95 percent** of]{.mark} its [**living
coral** by **2050**]{.mark} [should ocean temp]{.mark}eratures [increase
by]{.mark} the **[1.5 degrees]{.mark}** Celsius [**projected**
by]{.mark} climate [scientists]{.mark}. [Ocean acidification]{.mark},
resulting from higher concentrations of dissolved carbon dioxide, [is a
further threat to]{.mark} corals and plankton that form the basis of
[the marine food chain]{.mark}.]{.underline}

#### [Climate change]{.underline} drives [biodiversity loss]{.underline} -- rising temperatures, extreme weather, and invasive species.

**Greenpeace ND** -- (\"What are the effects of climate change on
biodiversity?\"; Greenpeace UK;
https://www.greenpeace.org.uk/challenges/wildlife-and-biodiversity/climate-change-biodiversity/;
Accessed 7-12-2025)//ILake-JuY

[Climate change]{.underline} is dramatically shaping our world today.
But its consequences are not limited to humans alone. It also [has
far-reaching effects on the world's biodiversity.]{.underline} Let\'s
explore how climate change and biodiversity are interlinked -- and how
tackling one can help the other.

Climate change is an undeniable reality that is dramatically shaping our
world today. Its impacts are profound, from rising temperatures and
melting ice caps to extreme weather. But its consequences are not
limited to humans alone. It also has far-reaching effects on the world's
biodiversity. This is often overlooked, yet it is interlinked.

[[In]{.mark} just over [40 years]{.mark}, [wildlife]{.mark} populations
[show]{.mark}ed a [dramatic **decline of 69%**]{.mark} on average. The
[IPCC reports]{.mark} that around 20-**[30%]{.mark}** of [**species
could die out** if]{.mark} global mean [temp]{.mark}eratures [go up by
1.5]{.mark}-2.5 [degrees]{.mark}.]{.underline} Yet the [[global
average]{.mark} surface temperature has [risen by]{.mark} roughly [1
degree]{.mark} Celsius]{.underline}. The data speaks for itself,
painting a clear picture of the current state of biodiversity and
climate change.

\<\<PARAGRAPH CONDENSED\>\>

The effects of climate change on biodiversity Biodiversity, or the
'diversity of life' on Earth, is essential for the planet's health and
survival. But the effects of climate change are becoming increasingly
clear. Extreme weather events are happening more often. The timing of
biological events is changing. Our wildlife is disappearing. But its
impacts are not felt the same across the planet, as some regions and
species are more at risk than others. Climate change is having a greater
impact on the poles, tropical rainforests, and coastal areas than
anywhere else in the world. This could have far-reaching consequences
for the planet's health; and our future. Rising global temperatures
leads to habitat loss on land and sea Rising global temperatures, also
known as 'global warming', is one of the most important aspects of
climate change. It refers to the increasing greenhouse gases, like CO2
and methane, in the atmosphere, which trap the sun's heat. This trapped
heat is related---directly or indirectly---to widespread habitat loss on
both land and in the sea. On the surface, polar ice caps and glaciers
are melting. This is causing sea levels to rise and encroach on coastal
habitats and the Arctic tundra. Not only this, but global rainfall
patterns are being affected as well. The changes in weather patterns can
lead to extreme environmental changes, like 'desertification'. This is
when wild habitats like grasslands or shrublands slowly turn into
deserts. In the oceans, CO2 concentrations are now higher than they have
been in the past 800,000 years. As a result, our seas are getting
warmer, and the oceans are becoming more acidic. These conditions are
known to cause mass coral bleaching. To date, scientists have identified
six coral bleaching events since 1998, most notably on the Great Barrier
Reef. This is of concern since coral reefs are one of the most
biodiverse and valuable ecosystems on Earth.

\<\<PARAGRAPH INTEGRITY RESUMES\>\>

Changing weather patterns disrupt seasonal lifecycles and events

[[C]{.mark}limate [c]{.mark}hange also affects biodiversity by
**[disturb]{.mark}ing** the timing of **[natural cycles]{.mark}** and

[migrate or]{.mark} when to [reproduce]{.mark}. So any big changes in

[grounds when]{.mark} the [food]{.mark} source [is scarce]{.mark}r.
[This]{.mark} can [**threaten their survival** as well as]{.mark} their
[breeding]{.mark} efforts.]{.underline}

[[Climate]{.mark} breakdown can also lead to an
**[unpredictable]{.mark}** outcome [for]{.mark} the functioning of

[c]{.mark}hange.]{.underline}

Extreme weather and frequent weather-related disasters harms wildlife

[[C]{.mark}limate [c]{.mark}hange is [causing extreme weather]{.mark}

**[more severe]{.mark}**.]{.underline} Such tragic events not only

We can also see the effects of [**weather-related** disasters on

the extreme weather [in 2022]{.underline} was very bad for UK wildlife.
[[Dried-up rivers]{.mark} and streams affected wild populations of

Climate change creates pathways for the introduction and spread of
invasive species

[The [geographic ranges]{.mark} of many animal and plant species have
been **[shifting]{.mark} from low to high** [latitudes in response to
c]{.mark}limate [c]{.mark}hange. This can [create]{.mark} new [pathways
for invasive species]{.mark} that are not native to a particular area.
And often, they are introduced by human activity.]{.underline}

When the climate changes, [invasive species]{.underline} can move into
new habitats and [[compete with native species]{.mark} for food, shelter
and space]{.underline}. [They]{.underline} can [[also introduce new
diseases]{.underline}]{.mark}. For example, [[red squirrel]{.mark}s used
to be a common]{.underline} sight [in the UK---the **only native**
squirrel **species**]{.underline}. But their [[population]{.mark} has
been [declining]{.mark}]{.underline} for many years
[[since]{.underline}]{.mark} the introduction of grey squirrels from
North America in the 1800s.

The larger [[grey squirrels]{.underline}]{.mark} are much better at
adapting to changing conditions than the smaller red squirrels. [They
are also known to [carry]{.mark} the '[**squirrelpox**']{.mark}
virus]{.underline}. [[Grey squirrels]{.mark} are [immune]{.mark} to this
disease, [but it kills]{.mark} the [red squirrels]{.mark}]{.underline}.
This causes population decline. Red squirrels are now only found in
Scotland, parts of northern England and Wales, and small islands near
England's southern coast.

It leads to an increase in human-wildlife conflict

Climate change also contributes to human-wildlife conflict. As wild
habitats shrink and food sources become scarce, animals are forced to
move closer and closer to human settlements. This leads to increased
interactions between humans and wildlife, often resulting in harm to
both parties. From the loss of crops and livestock to the loss of human
lives and animals involved. Besides, there is also the risk of disease
transmission in both animals and humans.

Human-wildlife conflict is an upcoming global issue. It poses a
significant challenge for biodiversity conservation, as well as for our
health and well-being.

It contributes to wildlife decline and extinction of species

[The extinction of species is perhaps the most worrying effect of
climate change on biodiversity. The most at-risk are those with
**limited climate ranges**, like polar bears and arctic
foxes]{.underline}. It is [also]{.underline} the case for [animals with
restricted habitats and small, isolated populations]{.underline}.
[Galapagos giant tortoises and marine iguanas are a few good
examples.]{.underline}

Our current estimates suggest that [wildlife populations have declined
by an average of 69% since 1970]{.underline}. And [[species]{.mark} are
[**disappearing** between 1,000 and **10,000 times** higher]{.mark}
[than what is natural]{.mark}.]{.underline} Other human activities, such
as deforestation and overfishing, are also cause for concern. The loss
of species not only reduces biodiversity, but it also indicates that the
ecosystem is breaking down. If we continue as usual, there is a risk of
global system failure unless we take urgent action. For example, if we
lose bees, we may lose all the plants that they pollinate. This will
then affect all the animals that eat those plants, including humans. A
world without bees could mean a rise in global food insecurity unless we
take urgent action.

### 2AC\-\--No Link

#### Seafloor mineral collection is [necessary]{.underline} and [ecologically sustainable]{.underline}.

In pure quantitative resource terms, deep-sea metals are thus optional
for net-zero pathways. [But [given how society]{.mark} is presently
[grappling with]{.mark} how to best [expand and diversify

requirements for large-scale electric vehicle deployment.

\<\<Table Omitted\>\>

In response, activists challenge the very idea that society requires any
significant new mining at all, often by calling instead for improved
recycling and a crusade against private automobile ownership. This
car-hating recycling-based circular economy platform rather elegantly
upholds traditional conservationist principles while dodging most
acknowledgement that the global shift towards more sustainable societies
might involve ecological tradeoffs. However, it is both incoherent and
incorrect. Specific [[proposals for reducing car ownership]{.mark} are
often **[unrealistic]{.mark}ally overoptimistic**, while cold, hard
[math suggests]{.mark} that [even]{.mark} a [vastly smaller global car
fleet]{.mark} would [still require]{.mark} [electric vehicle
replacements]{.mark} on the order of [at least **several hundred
million** electric cars]{.mark}, relative to the 30 million or so that
exist today. The quantitative [case for]{.mark} new [battery metal
mining is unshakeable]{.mark}.]{.underline}

As such, [climate hawks would do well to consider the environmental case
for deep-sea metals. [One]{.mark} particular [advantage is
adaptive]{.mark} [management]{.mark}. [Unlike]{.mark} a [surface
mine]{.mark} on land, [where]{.mark} many significant [ecological
impacts occur]{.mark} all [at once]{.mark} during the mine's initial
construction, the [fingerprint of nodule collection]{.mark} on the
seafloor [is **incremental** with]{.mark} every unit of [rocks
collected]{.mark}]{.underline}. [If scientists conclude that it is
important for collector vehicles to leave more nodules behind,
[operators can **adjust accordingly**]{.mark} even [**midway**
through]{.mark} [harvesting]{.mark} an area.]{.underline} If regulators
determine that underwater noise from nodule collection is more harmful
than anticipated, they can require technology improvements that reduce
impacts from that point forward. If collector vehicle technology
improves in ways that further minimize environmental risks, regulators
can compel all operators to adopt that technology.

At the most basic level, [we can imagine [forms of seafloor nodule
collection]{.mark} that **[tread extremely lightly]{.mark}** upon the
seafloor]{.underline}. Regulators are working with scientists and
aspiring operators to define initial precautionary thresholds for
dissolved metals, noise, light, and turbidity that nodule collectors
will in turn commit to meet. Across the conceivable spectrum of
approaches one can envision robots that only disturb sediment to half
the depth, that use dimmer onboard lights, or that eject sediment in a
controlled manner to greatly reduce the size of the plume in their wake.
[Advocacy by many opponents for seafloor mining bans that would
foreclose any of these possibilities hints at a **narrow-minded
aversion** that fixates more on the idea of collecting seabed metals
than it does on the actual impacts.]{.underline}

#### DSM is better environmentally and economically than land mining

**Haigler '12** \[Lauren; 7-16-2012; The American Security Project (ASP)
is a nonpartisan organization created to educate the American public and
the world about the changing nature of national security in the 21st
Century; American Security Project, \"The US Needs Domestic Access to
Rare Earth Minerals,\"
https://www.americansecurityproject.org/the-us-needs-domestic-access-to-rare-earth-minerals/\]

[[Mining for **rare earths in the deep-seabed** has **economic and
environmental advantages over land mining**.]{.mark}
Some [research]{.mark} has [suggested]{.mark} that [deep-sea
mining]{.mark} [will be cheaper than land]{.mark} mining and yield
higher profits. [Also, the]{.mark} seafloor [deposits]{.mark}
[are]{.mark} much [more concentrated]{.mark} than those on land [and
would]{.mark} therefore [take less processing to extract the minerals,
which is the most environmentally destructive part]{.mark} of mining.
New technology has also reduced the environmental risk of deep-sea
mining. More than one metal can be obtained at one deep-sea site. [Land
mines leave a substantial footprint but d]{.mark}eep-[s]{.mark}ea
[m]{.mark}ines [have no roads, surface ore-transport systems, or other
infrastructures.]{.mark}]{.underline}

[Jay Timmons, President and CEO of the National Association of
Manufacturers, explained the importance of domestic access to rare
earths in his testimony to the Senate Committee on Foreign Relations
supporting LOTS: "[**Without**]{.mark} those [**rare earth materials**,
**manufacturing**]{.mark} simply **[will not be able to
compete]{.mark}** and succeed [in the world marketplace]{.mark}... The
bottom line is if we can't access rare earth materials on the floor of
the sea, we're going to be put at a significant competitive
disadvantage."]{.underline}

The Senate has an opportunity to help jump-start the US rare earth
minerals market and create jobs in a way that is more environmentally
friendly than the House legislation. Plus, there are many, many more
advantages that would be afforded to the US economy and national
security through ratification of the treaty besides deep-sea mining.

#### Other countries are mining now in their continental shelves

**Comay and Keating-Bitonti '25** \[Laura; Caitlin; 5-14-2025; Laura
Comay is a Specialist for Natural Resources Policy in the Congressional
Research Service; Caitlin is a science policy analyst with diverse
experiences working with US congressional, US executive branch, and
foreign government decisionmakers; Congressional Research Service,
\"Critical Minerals on the U.S. Outer Continental Shelf: The Bureau of
Ocean Energy Management's Role and Issues for Congress,\"
https://www.congress.gov/crs-product/R48302\]

[[**Several countries have taken steps to mine for seabed minerals** on
their continental shelves. For example]{.mark}, the [Cook Islands,
India, Japan, and Norway]{.mark} have passed domestic laws related to
seabed mining activities in their national waters, invested in the
exploration of their continental shelves for marine minerals, and/or
developed technology for the purpose of commercial
recovery]{.underline}.

The Cook Islands Seabed Mineral Authority estimates that 6.7 billion
metric tons of polymetallic nodules---potato-shaped rocks lying on the
deep seafloor that may contain cobalt, copper, manganese, nickel, and
rare earth elements (REEs)---occur on the country\'s continental shelf.
The Cook Islands Seabed Minerals Authority estimates these nodules
contain 20 million metric tons of cobalt. In 2022, the Authority issued
three five-year licenses to explore for polymetallic nodules on the Cook
Islands continental shelf. As of April 25, 2025, the Cook Islands
government has allowed only for exploration of its seabed for
polymetallic nodules, not resource extraction. For more information
about seabed mining in the Pacific Islands region, see CRS In Focus
IF12974, *Seabed Mining Interests Across the Pacific Islands*, by
Caitlin Keating-Bitonti and Jared G. Tupuola.

[India\'s domestic legislation for offshore seabed mining, the Offshore
Areas Mineral (Development and Regulation) Act, was amended in 2023 to
include an auction method for allocating operating rights within Indian
waters.]{.underline} In November 2024, the Ministry of Mines identified
13 offshore mineral blocks for auction in three regions across India\'s
continental shelf. [Seven of these blocks, all located off Great Nicobar
Island in the eastern Indian Ocean, will be auctioned for polymetallic
nodules and crusts, which are expected to contain cobalt, iron, lead
manganese, nickel, and REEs.]{.underline} Some opponents of seabed
mining have reported that the Geological Survey of India conducted
limited observational studies off Great Nicobar Island.

In 2010, Japan experienced REE supply disruptions from China, which
controls more than 70% of the global market share in REEs. Japan has
since explored its continental shelf for seabed mineral deposits, in
accordance with a domestic 2007 ocean policy law. In 2017, a Japanese
government-owned mining company reportedly mined zinc and other minerals
from an inactive hydrothermal vent on Japan\'s continental shelf.
[[Japan\'s government also has invested in]{.mark} pumping [machinery to
extract deep-sea muds for REEs;]{.mark} researchers estimate these muds
could meet annual global demands for some REEs, such as yttrium,
europium, terbium, and dysprosium, for 30-60 years. Some [experts
speculate that seabed mining within Japan\'s]{.mark} national [waters
could shift it]{.mark} from being import dependent [to being a mineral
resources-producing country]{.mark}]{.underline}[.]{.mark}

In 2008, Norwegian geologists discovered a hydrothermal vent system
located on Norway\'s continental shelf that occurs along the Arctic
Mid-Ocean Ridge. Some geologists have speculated that economic
quantities of minerals (e.g., copper, zinc) occur in the deposits
surrounding the vent system. On January 9, 2024, the Norwegian
Parliament opened an area of its continental shelf for commercial-scale
mining activities. Following the Norwegian Parliament\'s decision, the
European Parliament passed a resolution expressing its concerns about
Norway opening an area of its continental shelf for mining. On December
1, 2024, Norway\'s Socialist Left Party blocked the government\'s plans
to offer the country\'s first deep-sea mining exploration permits in
early 2025 due to environmental concerns. Norway\'s Ministry of Energy
plans to hold the first licensing round in 2026, according to reporting.

that activists are citing [concludes]{.mark} that [nodules emit low
amounts of]{.mark} relatively [harmless]{.mark} alpha
[radiation]{.mark}, [which cannot even penetrate human
skin]{.mark}]{.underline}, and proposes that simply requiring workers to
wear an N95 mask would provide effective protection.

### Greenpeace Indict

#### Greenpeace bad and is false

Noel **Yaxley**, 20**21** -- Writer and researcher who regularly writes
publications. "We need to talk about Greenpeace," The Critic,
https://thecritic.co.uk/talk-greenpeace/.

Since the group re-emerged after a long hiatus in the wilderness ---
probably busy making hair shirts or shouting at petrol --- [they]{.mark}
[have tried]{.mark} every means at their disposal [to raise
awareness]{.mark} [that we are living in a supposed climate
emergency]{.mark}. [Their hapless]{.mark} Dad's Army [approach]{.mark}
to environmental activism managed to [[enrage]{.mark} and frustrate just
about [every sector of]{.mark} British [society]{.mark}]{.underline}.
From a blockade that obstructed newspaper printing presses to disrupting
the travel plans of millions of fed up commuters, the [group]{.mark}
managed to [alienate]{.mark} just about [anyone]{.mark} who may have
been receptive to their cause. [**[Hyperbolic statements]{.underline}**
didn't help]{.mark} either. [Founding]{.mark} member Roger Hallam
[[claimed "billions"]{.mark} will [die]{.mark}]{.underline} if we fail
to take immediate action. Rather [than]{.mark} bring people with them,
these **[[ridiculous stunts and statements]{.underline}]{.mark}** have
had the opposite effect.

Although Extinction Rebellion are the current torch bearers of
performative activism, the progenitors of contemporary environmental
virtue signalling are Greenpeace but it wasn't always this way.
Originally called "Don't Make a Wave Committee" the group was formed
fifty years ago in Vancouver, Canada with two clear goals --- the
protection of whales and the wholesale elimination of all nuclear
testing. In order to draw attention to the United States' testing of
nuclear weapons in the North Pacific, the group, headed by co-founder
Bob Hunter, chartered a vessel and headed towards the test site.
Although bad weather caused the ship to turn back, the stunt drew a lot
of attention. In 1971 they decided to rebrand, taking the name from the
fishing boat used in the protest --- "The Greenpeace".

[[They]{.mark} [adopt]{.mark} a [Malthusian mindset which **falsely
attributes over-population as**]{.mark} **the [primary cause of climate
change]{.mark}**.]{.underline}

It didn't take long for the group to be subverted. The long march
through the green institution was more of a sprint. [Greenpeace]{.mark}
was being **[[hijacked by radicals]{.underline}]{.mark}** [as early
as]{.mark} the mid [80's]{.mark}. Just ask Patrick Moore. Moore,
[an]{.mark} [original member]{.mark} of Greenpeace left the organisation
in 1986 saying " \[they\] took a sharp turn to the political left,"
[argu]{.mark}ing that [it had "evolved into]{.mark} an organisation of
[extremism and politically motivated agendas]{.mark}."

They are now active in over 50 countries. Boasting a worldwide annual
revenue of \$368 million dollars, they have become one of the most
powerful environmental organisations in the world. They quickly evolved
from a relatively obscure tranche of concerned environmentalists into a
monolithic ecological organisation [[advocat]{.mark}ing [more and more
**dangerous and ill-conceived** forms of direct
action]{.mark}]{.underline}. One infamous stunt involved draping a
banner over the 1,500 year old Nazca lines in Peru --- damaging the
earth around the U.N World Heritage site --- an act of cultural
desecration.

Greenpeace's current campaign takes aim at the growing problem of
plastic pollution. In a recently released video piles of plastic
detritus can be seen raining down on a computer generated Boris Johnson.
The amount of plastic seen in the clip is supposed to represent what we
export on an average day to developing countries. Whilst certainly a
problem --- exports of plastic waste reached 537,000 tonnes last year
--- Greenpeace are at least in part responsible for this voluminous
amount of rubbish.

The group has spent twenty years opposed to the building of waste
plants, otherwise known as incinerators. These plants dispose of rubbish
by burning waste material. Citing the potentially lethal by-products of
this industrial process --- dioxins --- Greenpeace Toxics campaigner
Mark Strutt has labelled the building of incinerators "dangerous and
irresponsible."

Whilst Strutt is correct --- incinerators do indeed produce dioxins ---
they are relatively small compared to other emission sources. In 2017
the annual dioxin emissions from waste plants was ten times lower than
that emitted from a single night of Bonfire celebrations on or around
November 5th.

An activist [organisation taking]{.mark} a [stand]{.mark}
[against]{.mark} [something]{.mark} [likely to improve our lives]{.mark}
is hardly news. But when this opposition [has]{.mark} potentially
**[[life-threatening ramifications]{.underline}]{.mark}**, it needs to
be addressed.

Golden Rice is the result of nine years of research. It is a form of
rice that has been genetically modified. It's an example of
bio-fortification --- a process whereby a crop is modified in order to
improve its nutritional value. The gold colour comes from beta carotene
--- a substance that converts into vitamin A in the body. Whilst
relatively unknown in the west, vitamin A deficiency is a life or death
issue in the developing world. According to Johns Hopkins, a lack of
this essential vitamin is responsible for an annual worldwide death toll
of as many as 3 million children. Those that don't suffer a slow painful
death through chronic malnutrition are left permanently blind. Golden
rice could help eradicate this deficiency in countries where the
condition is endemic.

The organisation is well known for its hostility to genetically modified
organisms (GMOs). So much so that 100 Nobel laureates signed an open
letter to the group calling for them to end their anti GMO campaign.
Their opposition has been persistent and strident. [Falsely
calling]{.mark} the rice a "[hoax]{.mark}" -claiming the funding for its
scientific research has diverted money away from the real problem ---
global poverty. According to Ed Regis, author of Golden Rice, "The rice
\[had\] been subjected to safety studies -toxicity and allergenicity
studies --- and studies on human consumption, including among American
adults and Chinese children. These have found it to be more effective in
providing vitamin A than spinach and almost as effective as pure beta
carotene oil itself."

[Both Extinction Rebellion and Greenpeace share]{.mark} an
[anti-humanist vision of progress]{.mark}

[Greenpeace's opposition]{.mark} to GMOs [brought condemnation]{.mark}
from one Harvard professor of genetics, George Church. In an interview
with Edge he said: "[A million lives are at stake every year]{.mark} due
to vitamin A deficiency, and Golden Rice was basically ready for use in
2002...every year you delay it, that's another million people dead.
[That's **[mass murder]{.underline}** on a high scale]{.mark}. In fact,
as I understand it there is an effort to bring them to trial at The
Hague for crimes against humanity. Maybe that's justified, maybe it
isn't."

Both Extinction Rebellion and Greenpeace share an anti-humanist vision
of progress. They adopt a Malthusian mindset which falsely attributes
over-population as the primary cause of climate change. It is us and our
apparent insatiable desire for capital that is the problem. But human
beings --- far from being what David Attenborough calls a "plague" ---
are a creative and intelligent species. [We are living in]{.mark} a
[period of great scientific achievement]{.mark}. Whilst we have not
quite reached the environmental nirvana many of these groups push for,
we have made great progress. [[On]{.mark} a [national level]{.mark}, we
have [decarbonised]{.mark} our eco[nomy faster than any other]{.mark}
major [economy]{.mark} in the last twenty years. Whilst globally**,
[deaths from climate-related disasters]{.mark} have [decreased by 90 per
cent]{.mark} [over]{.mark} the [past hundred
years]{.mark}**.]{.underline} With this in context [do]{.mark} [we
really need to have]{.mark} our [consciousness "raised" as]{.mark} so
many [activists claim]{.mark}?

[[Rather]{.mark} [than]{.mark} [advocating genuine environmental
reform]{.mark}, [Greenpeace]{.mark} have [regularly pursued **dangerous
campaigns**]{.mark} that [stand in **direct opposition to
progress**]{.mark}.]{.underline} Their [stubborn refusal to accept
scientific consensus and embrace technological innovation]{.mark} means
they **[are [harming far more than]{.mark} they are
[helping]{.mark}]{.underline}**.
